https://api.elsevier.com/content/article/pii/S0959652620322514 doi:10.1016/j.jclepro.2020.122204 1-s2.0-S0959652620322514 10.1016/j.jclepro.2020.122204 S0959-6526(20)32251-4 Unleashing the convergence amid digitalization and sustainability towards pursuing the Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs): A holistic review  Journal of Cleaner Production Journal rev 09596526 280 122204 122204 122204 text/plain 2021-01-20 20 January 2021 © 2020 Elsevier Ltd. All rights reserved. Elsevier Ltd. Del Río Castro, Gema González Fernández, María Camino Uruburu Colsa, Ángel 
                  The Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) within the United Nations 2030 Agenda emerged in 2015, becoming an unprecedented global compass for navigating extant sustainability challenges. Nevertheless, it still represents a nascent field enduring uncertainties and complexities. In this regard, the interplay between digitalization and sustainability unfolds bright opportunities for shaping a greener economy and society, paving the way towards the SDGs. However, little evidence exists so far, about a genuine contribution of digital paradigms to sustainability. Besides, their role to tackle the SDGs research gaps remains unexplored. Thus, a holistic characterization of the aforementioned topics has not been fully explored in the emerging literature, deserving further research. The article endeavors a twofold purpose: (1) categorizing the main SDGs research gaps; (2) coupled with a critical exploration of the potential contribution of digital paradigms, particularly Big Data and Artificial Intelligence, towards overcoming the aforesaid caveats and pursuing the 2030 Agenda. Ultimately, the study seeks to bridge literature gaps by providing a first-of-its-kind overview on the SDGs and their nexus with digitalization, while unraveling policy implications and future research directions. The methodology has consisted of a systematic holistic review and in-depth qualitative analysis of the literature on the realms of the SDGs and digitalization. Our findings evidence that the SDGs present several research gaps, namely: flawed understanding of complexities and interlinkages; design shortcomings and imbalances; implementation and governance hurdles; unsuitable indicators and assessment methodologies; truncated adoption and off-target progress; unclear responsibilities and lacking coordination; untapped role of technological innovation and knowledge management. Moreover, our results show growing expectations about the added value brought by digitalization for pursuing the SDGs, through novel data sources, enhanced analytical capacities and collaborative digital ecosystems. However, current research and practice remains in early-stage, pointing to ethical, social and environmental controversies, along with policy caveats, which merit additional research. In light of the findings, the authors suggest a first-approach exploration of research and policy implications. Results suggest that further multidisciplinary research, dialogue and concerted efforts for transformation are required. Reframing the Agenda, while aligning the sustainable development and digitalization policies, seems advisable to ensure a holistic sustainability. The findings aim at guiding and stimulating further research and science-policy dialogue on the promising nexus amid the SDGs and digitalization.

Conferences proceedings were not excluded but few references of this type have been included   Type of Analysis  Qualitative  Keywords/String Searches  Keywords/strings were used in the fields of “Title, Abstract and keywords”  Search focused on the SDGs (11647 results)  “Sustainable Development Goals” OR SDG∗ “2030 Agenda” Search focused on the convergence amid sustainability and digitalization (279 results):  Digitalization AND Sustainability Digital∗ AND “Sustainable Development” Digital∗ AND “Sustainable Development Goals” Digital∗ AND SDG∗ ICT∗ AND Sustainability ICT∗ AND “Sustainable Development Goals” ICT∗ AND SDG∗ “Big Data” AND SDG∗ “Artificial Intelligence” AND SDG∗ “Smart Cities” AND SDG∗ “ Tech∗ Innovation “AND “Sustainable Development”  Scientific Databases  Web of Science, Scopus, Elsevier (Science Direct), Emerald, Wiley, Springer, Sage Journals  Period  SDGs (2015–2019) Digitalization (2010–2019) (∗) A few references published in early 2020 (as of March 2020) have also been included. Review  Unleashing the convergence amid digitalization and sustainability towards pursuing the Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs): A holistic review Gema Del Río Castro a  ∗   María Camino González Fernández b   Ángel Uruburu Colsa b   a Universidad Politécnica de Madrid, Escuela Técnica Superior de Ingenieros Industriales, Calle José Gutiérrez Abascal, 2, 28006, Madrid, Spain Universidad Politécnica de Madrid Escuela Técnica Superior de Ingenieros Industriales Calle José Gutiérrez Abascal, 2 Madrid 28006 Spain  Universidad Politecnica de Madrid, Escuela Tecnica Superior de Ingenieros Industriales, Calle Jose Gutierrez Abascal, 2, 28006, Madrid, Spain  b Universidad Politécnica de Madrid, Escuela Técnica Superior de Ingenieros Industriales, Calle José Gutiérrez Abascal, 2, 28006, Madrid, Spain Universidad Politécnica de Madrid Escuela Técnica Superior de Ingenieros Industriales Calle José Gutiérrez Abascal, 2 Madrid 28006 Spain  Universidad Politecnica de Madrid, Escuela Tecnica Superior de Ingenieros Industriales, Madrid, Spain  ∗ Corresponding author.      Handling Editor: Prof. Jiri Jaromir Klemeš The Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) within the United Nations 2030 Agenda emerged in 2015, becoming an unprecedented global compass for navigating extant sustainability challenges. Nevertheless, it still represents a nascent field enduring uncertainties and complexities. In this regard, the interplay between digitalization and sustainability unfolds bright opportunities for shaping a greener economy and society, paving the way towards the SDGs. However, little evidence exists so far, about a genuine contribution of digital paradigms to sustainability. Besides, their role to tackle the SDGs research gaps remains unexplored. Thus, a holistic characterization of the aforementioned topics has not been fully explored in the emerging literature, deserving further research. The article endeavors a twofold purpose: (1) categorizing the main SDGs research gaps; (2) coupled with a critical exploration of the potential contribution of digital paradigms, particularly Big Data and Artificial Intelligence, towards overcoming the aforesaid caveats and pursuing the 2030 Agenda. Ultimately, the study seeks to bridge literature gaps by providing a first-of-its-kind overview on the SDGs and their nexus with digitalization, while unraveling policy implications and future research directions. The methodology has consisted of a systematic holistic review and in-depth qualitative analysis of the literature on the realms of the SDGs and digitalization. Our findings evidence that the SDGs present several research gaps, namely: flawed understanding of complexities and interlinkages; design shortcomings and imbalances; implementation and governance hurdles; unsuitable indicators and assessment methodologies; truncated adoption and off-target progress; unclear responsibilities and lacking coordination; untapped role of technological innovation and knowledge management. Moreover, our results show growing expectations about the added value brought by digitalization for pursuing the SDGs, through novel data sources, enhanced analytical capacities and collaborative digital ecosystems. However, current research and practice remains in early-stage, pointing to ethical, social and environmental controversies, along with policy caveats, which merit additional research. In light of the findings, the authors suggest a first-approach exploration of research and policy implications. Results suggest that further multidisciplinary research, dialogue and concerted efforts for transformation are required. Reframing the Agenda, while aligning the sustainable development and digitalization policies, seems advisable to ensure a holistic sustainability. The findings aim at guiding and stimulating further research and science-policy dialogue on the promising nexus amid the SDGs and digitalization.   Keywords Sustainability  Sustainable development goals (SDGs)  Digitalization  ICT  Big data  Artificial intelligence    1 Introduction Sustainability and digitalization stand as major trends shaping the economy and society. The nexus amid both domains foreshows outstanding, yet untapped, opportunities to foster a transformation towards sustainable development (Osburg and Lohrmann, 2017 ). Paradoxically, sustainability challenges endure unanswered (Köhler et al., 2019 ). Indeed, environmental and social landscapes are hugely worsening, showing a glaring sustainability gap (Seele, 2016a ). The Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) of the United Nations 2030 Agenda brightly emerged in 2015, as an unprecedented global consensus and governance compass for bridging the sustainability gap, by navigating the ecological, social and economic challenges that humanity faces. A burgeoning body of literature devoted to the SDGs has been emerging in the last five years. Studies mainly focus on specific design caveats (Saner et al., 2019 ; Swain, 2018 ; Weber, 2017 ) or implementation and monitoring hurdles (i.e. Caiado et al., 2018 ; Crespo-Cuaresma et al., 2018 ; Georgeson and Maslin, 2018 ; Salvia et al., 2019 ; Stafford-Smith et al., 2018 ) rather than on a critical overview of current research landscape. Thus far, there is no reference which comprehensively examines and categorizes all relevant research streams, gaps and controversies. Nevertheless, due to the novelty, multifaceted nature and extreme complexity of the 2030 Agenda, research is still incipient and fragmented, lacking sound science-policy dialogue and evidencing multiple controversies and open debates. Indeed, numerous implementation hurdles and uncertainties remain, being poorly covered in literature, thus deserving further understanding. Digitalization is heralded to be one of the most promising transformations for sustainability (Gouvea et al., 2018 ) towards tackling the SDGs (Sachs et al., 2019b ; Seele and Lock, 2017 ; Walker et al., 2019 ). However, regardless their underlying opportunities, digital paradigms such as Big Data and Artificial Intelligence, represent a nascent field lacking sufficient theoretical development and full of technological and non-technological challenges (Helbing et al., 2017 ; Hilbert, 2016 ; MacFeely, 2019 ). Controversies exist in terms of social and environmental sustainability (Akande et al., 2019 ; Kuntsman and Rattle, 2019 ; Scholz et al., 2018 ; Unwin, 2017 ). Only few studies address the relation amid digitalization and the SDGs, being a nexus which remains underrepresented in the scientific literature (i.e. GeSI, 2019 ; Kostoska and Kocarev, 2019 ; Tjoa and Tjoa, 2016 ; Vinuesa et al., 2019 ; Wu et al., 2018 ). Pioneer studies are mainly focused on mapping the contribution of ICTs for monitoring SDGs indicators within stand-alone “for good” projects in specific domains, instead of understanding the role of digital capacities for bridging the 2030 Agenda challenges. Therefore, little is known about whether and to what extend the huge opportunities brought by digitalization could be responsibly leveraged for good, or on the contrary, end to hamper the achievement of the 2030 Agenda (Fukuda-Parr and McNeill, 2019 ; Goralski and Tan, 2020 ). Through a systematic and comprehensive review of relevant literature and the latest developments in the fields of sustainability and digitalization, the purpose of this article is twofold: (1) identifying, categorizing and assessing the key research gaps related to the SDGs, setting an overview of current state-of-knowledge in the field, coupled with (2); a critical exploration of the role that digitalization and Information and Communication technologies (ICTs), particularly Big Data Analytics and Artificial Intelligence, could play towards overcoming those SDGs hurdles and enabling the 2030 Agenda. Current research only scratches the surface, and to our best knowledge, a holistic systematic review, able to untangle the full picture of the research gaps, implications and prospective directions related to the nexus amid the 2030 Agenda and the role of digitalization to tackled them, has not yet been delivered. Our holistic review aims at bridging current literature gaps, by providing critical insights about underlying research gaps of the 2030 Agenda, underscoring the opportunities and limitations that digitalization could bring for tackling the SDGs, while devising potential policy implications and future avenues of research. Therefore, this study contributes with a unique seminal reference for guiding researchers, practitioners and policy-makers from different disciplines in the understanding of both the 2030 Agenda challenges and the nexus between the SDGs and Digitalization, by theorizing a yet uncharted research field. Aligned with the aforementioned, this article addresses two main research questions:⁃ RQ1 - What are the overarching research gaps of the SDGs within the UN 2030 Agenda?    ⁃ RQ2 - Whether and, to what extent, digitalization and ICT paradigms can responsibly contribute to bridging the 2030 Agenda gaps towards pursuing the SDGs?      The study is structured as follows, and shown in Fig. 1  . The first section introduces the motivation of the article, followed by the second section focused on the methodology. The third and fourth sections tackle the literature review and analysis concerning the first research question. Particularly, the third section firstly provides an outline about sustainability and, subsequently, it introduces the Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) rationale. The fourth section addresses the SDGs implementation and governance hurdles. The fifth section is focused on the analysis of the convergence amid sustainability and digitalization, addressing the second research question. The sixth section covers a discussion where the research questions are answered in light of the previous review, beginning by summarizing the SDGs research gaps, followed by an exploration of the role of digital paradigms to bridge those caveats, closing with preliminary policy implications. Finally, the conclusion recapitulates the key findings, limitations of the study and future avenues which deserve further research.  2 Methodology The study has been undertaken through a systematic selection, in-depth review and synthesis of relevant literature over the last 10 years, as broadly acknowledged review approach (i.e. Aldieri et al., 2019 ). The review is based on qualitative methods, considering the most relevant sources of information from all relevant bodies of knowledge, primarily peer-reviewed articles in JCR journals, but also relevant pieces of grey literature, since some of the phenomena remain poorly explored in the scientific literature. It has been focused on identifying, clustering by themes and critical analyzing existing debates related to sustainability, the SDGs, and the convergence amid sustainability and digitalization (see Fig. 2  ), in order to generate both a big picture and detailed insights about the rationale and the policy, practical and research implications behind those discussions. The methodology followed is summarized in Table 1  . Two different screening processes for identifying scientific studies were performed: one focused on the SDGs solely, and the other focused on the interrelation amid sustainability, the SDGs, and digitalization. Different databases were explored in order to have a first understanding of the volume and adequacy of publications available. Afterwards, relevant journals and authors featured for more articles and citations were identified, leading to a screening refinement. Considering the novelty of the research realms, the publication period was narrowed to 2015–2019 for the SDGs (adopted in 2015) and 2010–2019 for the sustainability-digitalization domain (which began to emerge in the 2010). A few references published in early 2020 (as of March 2020) have also been included. Due to the multidimensional nature of the studied domains, subject areas were not restricted excessively, including ICTs, environmental, social, policy, business science and research. A parallel screening was conducted to identify relevant publications from practitioners, relevant initiatives (i.e. the Global e-Sustainability Initiative “GeSI”) and Multilateral Organisations (i.e. United Nation Agencies), as well as insights created through the different forums that have been forging the ground of knowledge. Once selected the documents, a first analysis of the abstract/executive summary was conducted to ensure relevance. Afterwards, the full text of relevant publications were profoundly analyzed, summarized and clustered according to central concepts and research themes. Through snowball analysis other contributions and refinements were added to further development of some of the sections.  3 The rationale of The Sustainable Development Goals within the Un 2030 agenda In this section, a brief introduction of the sustainability context is provided, followed by an overview on the SDGs rationale, including background, design shortcomings and imbalances. 3.1 An introduction to sustainability Sustainability has become a moral and economic imperative, since nature, society and businesses are strongly intertwined, affecting the valuation of any organization (Kiron and Unruh, 2018 ). Thus, success is no longer measured only in economic terms (Atos, 2018 ; UN Global Compact-Accenture, 2019 ). Paradoxically, the world seems to be far from a sustainable place. The negative footprints, climate crisis, severe biodiversity loss, pollution, inequality and social tensions, together with unsustainable behaviors remain or worsen (Kopnina, 2020 ; Osburg and Lohrmann, 2017 ), leading to growing threats (Priyadarshini and Abhilash, 2018 ; Yingchun et al., 2018 ). Progress to date has not prevented the damage on the environment (Leal-Filho et al., 2018 ; Sabau, 2020 ). Indeed, environmental-related threats outrank the rest of global risks for third consecutive year (WEF, 2019a ). The Anthropocene trajectory has placed strong pressure on earth systems, due to the supremacy of economic growth at the expense of environmental and social degradation (Kopnina, 2020 ; Steffen et al., 2015 ), causing supply problems, along with economic and ecological damage (Kopnina, 2020 ; Osten et al., 2017 ). A conspicuous stagnation of sustainable development is revealed, a divide between intention and poor adoption, known as the “sustainability gap” (Dyllick and Muff, 2016 ; Seele, 2016a ). Numerous experts argue that the concept of sustainable development (SD) is an oxymoron (Spaiser et al., 2017 ), powerful but idealistic umbrella (Shahadu, 2016 ) made of multiple competing interests, demanding sound management and methodologies. The boundaries of the sustainability conception have been built without systemic perspective (Lozano, 2015 ). Decades of fragmented research and divergent theories have contributed to a feeble status, full of misconstructions. Even when sustainability science has impacted on the research landscape, by shedding light and bridging gaps amid disciplines, it remains in its infancy (Elsevier, 2015 ), as a “niche field” (Messerli et al., 2019 ). Sustainability problems endure unanswered (Köhler et al., 2019 ), being considered wicked (Capasso et al., 2019 ) and deserving new approaches, particularly in the social sphere (Kirschke and Newig, 2017 ). Significant efforts have been devoted to define sustainability, supported by vast literature. However, the concept is controversial (Köhler et al., 2019 ) and it has been so often misused, that the actual meaning is weakened. Even when it could allow different views, current lack of consensus undermines its realization (Waas et al., 2011 ). Attaining sustainability will require interactive efforts among disciplines, which have not yet embraced, since research remains silo-based (Bergman et al., 2018 ). Thus, an integrated and consistent approach is urgently needed (McMichael et al., 2003 ). A glimpse of history Foremost concepts of sustainability and SD were formed in the beginning of the XXth  century onwards (De Oliveira Neto, 2018 ), even though early notions are much older (Waas et al., 2011 ). SD concept became popular and gained momentum with the publication of the mythic “Brundtland report” (WCED, 1987 ), but even nowadays it remains poorly understood (Mensah, 2019 ; Shi et al., 2019 ), challenging policy-makers and scientists (Ramcilovic-Suominen and Pülzl, 2018 ). From then on, sustainability has inspired intense and controversial debates, evolving together with a sound body of theories and myriads of definitions. Two relevant milestones were put forth by Freeman (1984) , author of the “theory of the stakeholders” and (Elkington, 1997 ) who proposed the renowned “triple bottom-line”. Another iconic milestone was the “Earth Charter” (2000), a transformative framework towards a sustainable, just and peaceful society. Rockström et al. (2009)  coined the “planetary boundaries” frame, where sustainability is conceived as the ability to stay within 9 ecological carrying-capacity thresholds. During the 1980s ecological sustainability was the main focus (Osburg, 2017 ). This narrow emphasis on ecology caused counterproductive conflicts with the dominant social and economic realms, jeopardizing sustainability as a whole (Bergman et al., 2018 ). In the 1990s, the perspective evolved, pursuing reconciliation amid environmental, economic and social dimensions based on the ‘triple bottom line’, coined by Elkington (1997) . Currently, the climate crisis outranked the rest of ecological concerns. Recently, new dimensions have been added, namely the cultural aspects and a 4th pillar on good governance (Shi et al., 2019 ). Daly et al. (1995)  put forth a novel classification: weak, intermediate, and strong sustainability based on different types of capital. In weak sustainability, economic, natural, and social capitals are substitutes. In intermediate sustainability, natural capital could be partially substituted. Strong sustainability is featured by an economic vision able to preserve natural and social capital (De Oliveira Neto, 2018 ). Neumayer (2010)  enhanced this definition based on indicators such as: “genuine savings, sustainable economic welfare indexes, ecological footprints”. Shi argues that strong sustainability should be the prefer concept. However, it remains unaddressed in the literature, lacking specific actions (Osburg, 2017 ). Chang et al. (2017)  remarks the relevance of the Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) while Hazen et al. (2016)  examines theories such as the social capital, the institutions, agency and actor-network. Lastly, the UN 2030 Agenda adopted in 2015 (the Agenda hereafter ), constitutes the most ambitious, goal-based and universal blueprint for sustainability ever in the history of humanity.  Evolution of the sustainability concept Defining sustainability is highly complex, due to its holistic character, normative dimension, scale dependence and multiple facets. Hence, a universally concept remains “elusive” and controversial (Bergman et al., 2018 ; Köhler et al., 2019 ; Waas et al., 2011 ; White, 2013 ). Sustainability entails both means and ends, it is a process and a destination, a sort of philosophy with diverse communities (Johnston et al., 2007 ) stemming from multiple fields (Zamora-Polo and Sánchez-Martín, 2019 ). Boundaries are not clear, going beyond theory and predisposed to interpretation (Köhler et al., 2019 ). Therefore, it cannot be fully empirically proven without context-based interpretation (Waas et al., 2011 ). Caradonna and Ingram (2015) , Glavic and Lukman (2007) , Pater and Cristea (2016)  and Waas et al. (2011) , provide reviews about the evolution of the concept and terminology, which has been classified into functional definitions associated to “sustainable development” and systemic approaches (Pater and Cristea, 2016 ). Köhler et al. (2019)  take stock of research trends and future avenues in the field of sustainability transitions. Despite lacking a universally-agreed definition, most scholars concur on fundamental principles in which sustainability is founded, namely: normativity, equity, integration and dynamism (Waas et al., 2011 ). Sustainability brings a notion of durability and futurity, harmonic co-existence of nature and humanity, preservation, equality and fairness, respect and protection of what is value, equitable and efficient resource allocation and environmentalism (Basiago, 1995 ; Costanza and Patten, 1995 ). Resilience (Roostaie et al., 2019 ) and trust (Osburg, 2017 ; Wenzel, 2017 ) are also highlighted in recent studies together with de-growth (Caradonna and Ingram, 2015 ) and circularity (Antikainen and Bocken, 2019 ; Schroeder et al., 2019 ; Weissbrod and Bocken, 2017 ). The most agreed concept relies on the balanced articulation of three pillars: social, environmental and economic. Munasinghe (2004)  suggested that sustainability can be regarded as the process to improve human progress while maintaining the resilience of the economic, social and environmental systems. Environmental sustainability is linked to the ecosystems capacity to preserve their purposes in the long term. Economic Sustainability is perceived as a “normative concept” related to the capacity of efficiently and responsibly optimizing the use of available resources to maximize profitability over time (Osburg, 2017 ). Social Sustainability entails society’s willingness to endure community well-being, equity and intergenerational justice, interrelated with trust and ethical behavior (Osburg, 2017 ). Boyer et al. (2016)  underscores that the social domain remains overlooked and suggest applying a context-based systemic approach to sort out wicked problems associated with sustainability. Osten et al. (2017)  states that even in common-pool resource systems characterized by social dilemmas and conflicting interests, sustainability is possible. Wenzel (2017)  set forth the “System Capacity Horizon (SCH)” meaning that sustainability is an evolving concept, a role model for success spiral, defining a “target function of life” constrained by system boundaries (ecological, social or financial). Emerging schools of thought claim for an urgent major transformation to overcome inertia, de-growth progress (Caradonna and Ingram, 2015 ) and shift towards a “conscious capitalism” (Mackey and Sisodia, 2013 ). Martinuzzi and Schönherr (2019)  analyse corporate sustainability from the early philanthropic focus to the future data-driven, systemic, goal and impact-based frameworks embedded within the organisations purpose. Indeed, sustainability should be regarded from a holistic, systemic, inter-disciplinary and forward-looking perspective (Ben-Eli, 2018 ) keeping a balance between development and preservation of nature and communities (Kates, 2005 ). Scholz et al. (2018)  conceives a systemic perspective based on: “transdisciplinary inquiry, systemic limits, inter-intragenerational justice”. Conventional theories and fragmented constructs are not sufficient to trigger the desirable changes towards reconciling progress, people and planet. Novel holistic models are required to embed added-value sustainability in the very heart and purpose of every organisation (Bocken et al., 2014 ; Patala et al., 2016 ) and human being.   3.2 The background of the Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) In 2015, two historical global agreements came into life, bringing unprecedented consensus, for making true a sustainable inclusive development within the planetary and social justice boundaries: the COP21 Paris Agreement (UNFCCC, 2015 ) together with the adoption of the resolution “Transforming our World: the 2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development”  (UN, 2015a ). Both represent a strategic compass and outstanding opportunity, not exempt of challenges, for building up an inclusive and sustainable future by the 2030 horizon. The 2030 Agenda is made of 17 goals and 169 targets supported over 5 pillars (planet, people, peace, prosperity, partnerships (see Fig. 3  ). It represents a promising blueprint for curbing excruciatingly “anthropogenic effects on the planet” (Osburg and Lohrmann, 2017 ), pursuing global governance and transnational policy coherence (Dalby et al., 2019 ; Kanie and Biermann, 2017 ). Building on the Millennium Development Goals (Sachs, 2012 ) and result of a broad participatory process (Saito et al., 2017 ), the Agenda aspires to be action-oriented, indivisible, multidisciplinary, inclusive and universally applicable, by integrating the three pillars of sustainability: economic, social and environmental (Biermann et al., 2017 ; Cheong, 2017 ; EU, 2019a ; Kanie and Biermann, 2017 ; Osburg and Lohrmann, 2017 ). It fosters goal-based and non-binding governance, relying on a flexible compliance nature (Biermann et al., 2017 ), which will require unique global cooperation (Dalby et al., 2019 ). The SDGs are carriers conveying interventions and policy constructions, advocating for priorities and values, and mobilizing action towards them (Ala-Uddin, 2019 ; Fukuda-Parr and McNeill, 2019 ). The Agenda aims at comprising relevant trends, such as the fourth industrial revolution, the impulse for a circular economy and responsible use of resources, the equality and gender dimension or the need for enhancing trust. It offers a novel perspective to transform global needs into solutions to societal challenges (WBCSD, 2019a ), evolving towards a strong sustainability that preserves natural and social capitals within the planetary boundaries (Holden et al., 2014 ). The SDGs, theoretically, represent a new future for net-value impact-oriented sustainability in the purpose of any organisation (Martinuzzi and Schönherr, 2019 ). All in all, the Agenda is an exceptional and ambitious attempt to reconcile progress and sustainability by building a “sustainable world leaving no one behind” . Facing such an enormous challenge will require cross-scale collaboration from all stakeholders and countries (Dalby et al., 2019 ; Florini and Pauli, 2018 ; Vazquez-Brust et al., 2020 ), as well as policy integration and coherence (Saito et al., 2017 ; Valencia et al., 2019 ). Nevertheless, almost five years after its adoption, numerous critical voices are rising against the current scheme, underscoring multiple limitations and drawbacks, which justify further dialogue to improve this blueprint for humanity. The SDGs will not be attained unless urgent measures are taken and sustainable transitions are triggered (Walsh et al., 2020 ). A disruptive transformation of the global economic and social systems is needed to achieve both the Agenda and the COP-21 agreement (Horan, 2019 ; Stevens and Kanie, 2016 ; UN, 2019d ). We explore as follows, the relevance of articulating the Agenda as a systemic network of goals.  3.3 The SDGs as a systemic network of goals Goals and targets within the Agenda are closely intertwined and can be perceived as a highly interconnected and systemic network, as acknowledged in the literature (Barbier and Burgues, 2017 ; Cutter, 2015 ; Dalby et al., 2019 ; Griggs et al., 2017 ; Griggs et al., 2014 ; Hall et al., 2018 ; Le Blanc et al., 2017 ; Le Blanc, 2015 ; Liu et al., 2018 ; Liu et al., 2015 ; Mensah, 2019 ; Miola et al., 2019 ; Niestroy, 2016 ; Nilsson et al., 2018 ; Nilsson et al., 2016 ; Schmidt et al., 2017 ; Stafford-Smith et al., 2017 ; UN, 2018b ; Weitz et al., 2019 ; Weitz et al., 2018 ; Zhou and Moinuddin, 2017 ). However, synergies and trade-offs among goals are poorly understood and hardly reflected in policy, calling for further research, towards delivering a systemic-approach. Research on interactions is a fast-growing domain, which can be undertaken by applying a sectorial approach (i.e. Griggs et al., 2017 ) or an extensive one, as a tool for prioritizing actions (i.e. Weitz et al., 2018 ). Manifold strategies exist, although five main methodologies can be highlighted: linguistic, literature, expert judgement, quantitative and modelling approaches (Miola et al., 2019 ). Saito et al. (2017)  provides an overview of studies focused on SDGs interlinkages. Stafford-Smith et al. (2017)  research interactions for multi-sectorial and transnational implementation. Yonehara et al. (2017)  and Allen et al. (2019a)  underscore the key role of evaluation. Nilsson et al. (2018)  compile insights from a conceptual framework for mapping interactions using a defined typology and characterization approach. The creation of sound models to understand the interactions is tackled by several experts (Collste et al., 2017 ; Khalili et al., 2017 ; Shahrier et al., 2017 ) while others devote their research to links among specific Goals (Coopman et al., 2016 ; Fuso-Nerini et al., 2018 ; Saraswat et al., 2017 ; Schmidt et al., 2017 ; Singh et al., 2018 ; Velis et al., 2017 ). Allen et al. (2019b)  argues that uncertainties exist regarding trade-offs. Pradhan et al. (2017)  examines synergies and trade-offs between pairs of SDGs in 227 countries, classifying the goals according to interlinks, concluding that the SDG12 is the Goal presenting more negative interactions, with 10 goals (SDG 1–7, SDG 9–10, SDG17), which could hamper sustainable consumption and production. Environmental-focused goals often conflict with those related to human or economic development, particularly in developed countries with intense footprints. Indeed, Scherer et al. (2018)  analyse the interaction between SDG1 (poverty), SDG10 (inequality) and environmental goals (SDG13, SDG15, SDG6) regarding consumption across 166 countries, concluding that promoting social goals usually leads to increase environmental impacts. Barbier and Burges (2019)  study trade-offs between net-gains reached on the SDG1 and the rest of goals, evidencing progress at the expense of environmental-based goals (SDG11-15). Pothen and Welsch (2019)  argue that advances in economic growth cause an increase of material footprint, causing environmental stress. Nevertheless, highly developed countries are able to decoupling growth from material consumption. Hence, quantitative interactions should be well understood to inform prioritization policies. Griggs et al. (2017)  identify positive synergies among several goals (SDG2, 3, 7, 14), by applying expert knowledge. Mainali et al. (2018)  apply network analysis and the Advanced Sustainability Analysis (ASA) framework in South Asia and Sub-Saharan Africa, proving synergies and trade-offs amid the SDG1, 2, 6, 7. Fuso-Nerini et al. (2018)  and Fader et al. (2018)  study the water-energy-food nexus. Von Stechow et al. (2016)  analyse trade-offs between Climate Action (SDG13) and the rest of SDGs, underscoring that curbing energy demand is crucial across the goals. McCollum et al. (2018)  underline remaining knowledge gaps regarding the interlinkages of the energy-based goal (SDG7) with other goals and across contexts, uncovering dependencies (relating to time, geography, governance, technology, and directionality). Hall et al. (2018)  claim that partnerships (SDG17) and climate action (SDG13) are enablers for other goals. The integrated management of water resources (SDG6) is crucial to reach the rest of targets, while the health-goal (SDG3) is influenced by the rest of goals. Thus cross-fertilization and collaboration among disciplines is needed. Obersteiner et al. (2016)  devote research to the land-food nexus and related trade-offs among environmental conservation initiatives and food prices. Sustainable Consumption and Production policies could mitigate trade-offs and should be central in formulating SDGs strategies with systemic approach. Kroll et al. (2019)  analyse the evolution of synergies and trade-offs amid goals, underscoring the role of innovation to create a “virtuous cycle of progress”. They conclude that positive synergies are found for the SDGs 1, 3, 7, 8 and 9, while pervasive trade-offs remain for SDGs 11, 13, 14, 16, 17. Mantlana  and Maoela  (2019) address the interlinkages of the SDG9 with social inclusion (SDG2, 11) and environmental issues (SDG 6, 13, 15), concluding that there exist both relevant synergies and trade-offs, between the SDG 6, 13, 15, which should be acknowledged by policy. Dolley et al. (2020)  denounce ignored trade-offs related to food security and poverty within peri-urban dynamics, as a result of non-representative aggregate data and top-down approaches. Scharlemann et al. (2020)  argue that current assessment do not focus on environmental-human interactions which actually influence most SDGs and should be considered. McCollum et al. (2018) , Sachs et al. (2019a)  and Schmidt-Traub et al. (2019)  criticize that spillovers across countries are pervasive and remain ignore. Dawes (2019)  argue that the SDG network is not self-consistent, therefore indirect trade-offs could hinder the realization of some Goals, while targeted allocated network effects, even when unevenly distributed, could be leveraged to achieve them. Thus, the literature reveals severe gaps in terms of methodology, confirming a fragmented scenario, where different authors reach diverse, sometimes contradictory outcomes, depending on the focus and context of study, therefore, further understanding and better tools are required. Experts claim for a “whole-of-government” approach to fully reflect the interlinkages avoiding negative transboundary effects (Miola et al., 2019 ; UN, 2018b ). A science-based qualitative nexus approach of both direct and indirect relations is suggested by Karnib (2017a)  although quantitative approaches on top of bottom-up views are also relevant (Karnib, 2017b ). Fusing quantitative and qualitative approaches is essential to better understand the links, and subsequently, to harness synergies while minimizing trade-offs (Obersteiner et al., 2016 ) and spill-overs, strengthening cross-sector and transnational cooperation (Breuer et al., 2019 ; Miola et al., 2019 ; Schneider at al., 2019 ; Weitz et al., 2019 ). Indeed, incentivizing cross-cutting relations across sectors and goals plays a crucial role in the Agenda operationalisation (Nilsson et al., 2016 ). Van Soest et al. (2019)  propose combining methods such as expert surveys, integrated assessment models from different disciplines and text mining, to enhance the results while overcoming underrepresented social and human issues and reinforcing a suitable representation of geographies and sectors. Requejo-Castro et al. (2020)  explore the interlinkages related to the SDG6 by means of Bayesian Networks, using data from 179 countries, proving the robustness of this analytical approach and the relevance of integrating a multi-sectorial approach. Therefore, new measurement methodologies (Sachs et al., 2019a ) and research approaches leveraging novel technologies, would enhance our understanding of interlinkages, making possible to translate them into better and coherent policies for SD (Breuer et al., 2019 ; Obersteiner et al., 2016 ; Schneider et al., 2019 ). Notwithstanding, complexities and uncertainties need to be tackled, concerning the different scales of implementation and context-specific actions (Allen et al., 2018 ; Georgeson and Maslin, 2018 ; Griggs et al., 2013 ; McCollum et al., 2018 ) demanding further research and policy efforts (Barbier and Burges, 2019 ; McCollum et al., 2018 ; Zimm et al., 2018 ), as for integration (Miola et al., 2019 ).  3.4 Design shortcomings of the SDGs The Agenda results of a complex policy process instead of a science-based dialogue, leading to a diluted ambition when setting the final targets (Fukuda-Parr and McNeill, 2019 ). Thus, the rational seems weak and the language remains complex and multi-layer (Ala-Uddin, 2019 ). Moreover, related provisions are feeble (Biermann et al., 2017 ), containing caveats and inconsistencies (Spaiser et al., 2017 ; Swain, 2018 ), which erode its credibility and jeopardize its achievement (Georgeson and Maslin, 2018 ; Saito et al., 2017 ). Even when, experts agree in the pertinence of the 17 SDGs at macro level, when downscaling to the target level, shortcomings are revealed. Only 29% of the targets are well-developed (ICSU, 2015 ), most are narrow, not representative and difficult to measure hindering progress or demanding additional regulatory efforts. Moreover, a clear implementation approach is missing (Horton, 2015 ). Despite the pretended holistic ambition, goals and targets are not well-connected, remaining inspired by a silo-based philosophy (Bergman et al., 2018 ) dominated by the economic dimension (Ala-Uddin, 2019 ; Pogge and Sengupta, 2015 ; Stevens and Kanie, 2016 ), and weakening the realization of the Goals. The new compass for sustainability is neither critical with the status-quo nor transformative enough (Stevens and Kanie, 2016 ). It should be ethically rooted, ambitious and disruptive and not just rewording old-fashioned models of progress (Dalby et al., 2019 ; Moore, 2015 ). Social, human and environmental values are insufficiently considered, since economic growth overweighs the discourse. Inclusivity is quite feeble (Dalby et al., 2019 ; Stevens and Kanie, 2016 ; Ulbrich et al., 2019 ; Weber, 2017 ), favoring commercial and political interests and omitting relevant issues such as political freedom or democracy (Ala-Uddin, 2019 ). Foundations for balancing inter-generational, intra-generational equity or social justice are also lacking (Spijkers, 2018 ; Terama et al., 2019 ; Vasconcellos Oliveira, 2018 ). Current Agenda scheme overlooks the ecological and relational dimensions of inclusiveness (Gupta and Vegelin, 2016 ). Furthermore, it has been criticized for being reductionist (Priyadarshini and Abhilash, 2018 ), failing to disrupt harmful structures (Hawkes and Buse, 2019 ) along with for numerous contradictions (Dalby et al., 2019 ), since it supports conflicting elements leading to a difficult implementation with uncertain impacts (Crespo-Cuaresma et al., 2018 ; Vasconcellos Oliveira, 2018 ). A narrative of change is missing (Costanza et al., 2016 ; Ostrom, 2014 ). Priorities of all the stakeholders are not reflected, showing a low balance between local and global scales. Roles and responsibilities are diffused without obligations (Spangenberg, 2017 ). Some authors argue that it seems more a wish list rather than a consistent set of smart goals (Diaz-Sarachaga et al., 2018 ; Pogge and Sengupta, 2015 ). Indeed, the Agenda seems disconnected of recognized methodologies and trends on the sustainability sphere (Lempert, 2017 ). A critical view is needed to impede conventional narratives which exacerbate inequalities (Ala-Uddin, 2019 ). Saner et al. (2019)  suggest revisiting the Agenda for making the most of prior advances and existing international agreements and including overlooked issues. Spijkers (2016)  claims for a more prominent impulse to sustainability, allowing cross-fertilization among disciplines and alignment with international frames. Stevens and Kanie (2016)  plea for transforming the idea of development towards systemic approaches. Given their relevance for pursing a truly sustainable and inclusive future, as follows we summarize the controversies regarding the imbalances behind the Agenda.  3.5 Imbalances amid dimensions and underrepresented issues As aforementioned, even when the Agenda aims at being universal and integrative, numerous scholars denounce that the economic development remains the main cornerstone, attesting imbalances and notable financial inertia (Ala-Uddin, 2019 ; Lempert, 2017 ; Priyadarshini and Abhilash, 2018 ; Stevens and Kanie, 2016 ; Swain, 2018 ; Weber, 2017 ). Besides, commonly accepted international agreements on the mentioned domains are feebly considered (Saner et al., 2019 ) and relevant elements are omitted (Dalby et al., 2019 ). Risks are not appropriately reflected, neither are resilience and accountability (REDS-SDSN, 2017 ). Environmental and social aspects are poorly covered (Allen et al., 2016 ), proving strong limitations in terms of inequality, ecosystems, poverty, consumption and production and governance. An absence of ecology and human rights (Pogge and Sengupta, 2015 ), as well as, insufficient representation of cultural values are clearly evidenced. Despite its relevance, migration appears as a missing goal (Dalby et al., 2019 ). The health goal does not cover transboundary problems such as global pandemics or climate impacts on well-being (Dalby et al., 2019 ). Furthermore, the education-related goal is ambiguous (Kopnina, 2020 ; Walid and Luetz, 2018 ) and related indicators could hinder advancement (Dalby et al., 2019 ). Certainly, it is generally agreed that the environment is the weakest dimension, whereas economic-focused goals (i.e. SDG8 and SDG9) are oversized at the expenses of the ecological-based (i.e. SDG12, SDG14, SDG15, SDG6), which indeed are the lagging ones. Particularly, the SDG12 presents a narrow scope, predominantly focused on efficiency and poor ambition, being unable to reflect the relevance of sustainable consumption and production (Gasper et al., 2019 ) and neglecting guidance on transformation (Bengtsson et al., 2018 ). Indeed, environmental targets were diluted during the Agenda negotiation, discarding truly transformative goals or strong implementation means (Elder and Olsen, 2019 ), then resulting feeble and inconsistent (Spaiser et al., 2017 ). The role of ecosystem services should be better embedded (Macellari et al., 2018 ; Spijkers, 2016 ), together with the integration of natural capital as a foundation of the development cycle (Lempert, 2017 ; Martinez-Harms et al., 2018 ; Vasseur et al., 2017 ). Furthermore, the contribution of soil science is not well considered (Bouma et al., 2019 ) and the water-energy-food-climate nexus is absent. Giannetti et al. (2020)  compare the SDGs with the model of capitals and the “five sectors sustainability model” (5SenSu) attesting an unbalance distribution, where the environmental capital is reduced, thus claiming for a good balance. Lobos-Alva and Hoff (2017)  advise to integrate the planetary boundaries, coined by Rockström et al (2009) , within national strategies, identifying vulnerable and highly productive contexts for supporting transformation, conceived from a systemic vision (Bengtsson et al., 2018 ). Definitely, uncertainties exist about the true potential of the Agenda to tackle sustainability challenges (Leal-Filho et al., 2019a ) in comparison with previous tools such as the “Earth Chapter” (Earth Chapter, 2000 ), considered more advanced, balanced and integrated. An imbalanced and economic-driven Agenda would not be able to reduce inequality (Pogge and Sengupta, 2015 ). On the contrary, it could aggravate current unsustainability landscape (Kopnina, 2016 ). Besides, many SDGs deserve better and further attention (Salvia et al., 2019 ). The imbalance is strongly derived of the lack of integration, since economic and social progress could hinder environmental goals (Scherer et al., 2018 ). Ethical approaches considering all relevant dimensions are needed for moving beyond the status-quo (Kopnina, 2016 ). Therefore, interactions should be understood and acknowledged and all dimensions should be better balanced (Bergman et al., 2018 ) and seamless integrated (Cutter, 2015 ) reflecting the vision of strong sustainability. The following section discusses the huge challenges which have to be tackled for wisely implementing and govern such an ambitious, multifaceted and integrative agenda.   4 Implementation and governance of The Sustainable Development Goals within the Un 2030 agenda 4.1 An overview of the glaring implementation challenges Implementing the SDGs poses serious defies, regarding planning, monitoring, assessment and financing (Dalby et al., 2019 ; Georgeson and Maslin, 2018 ) together with related policy challenges (Kanie and Biermann, 2017 ; Stevens and Kanie, 2016 ). The Goals are born universal, but need to be translated locally, into national plans, which depend on variable priorities across geographies (Allen et al., 2016 ; Salvia et al., 2019 ; Stafford-Smith et al., 2018 ). Translating the SDGs into local contexts requires clear, long-term vision supported by policy targets (Galli et al., 2018 ) along with investments. Indeed, present allocated level of investment is not sufficient to address the Agenda, revealing a financing gap (Lagoarde-Segot, 2020 ) and the urgency to mobilize further resources (Barua, 2019 ). Therefore, such implementation remains a burdensome task, full of complex challenges (Caiado et al., 2018 ). Nevertheless, it also represents an opportunity to navigate SD challenges (Leal-Filho et al., 2019a ), and align local and global needs supported by with science-based systemic frameworks (Stafford-Smith et al., 2018 ). Enhancing the means of implementation and governance to overcome hurdles and accelerate progress is subject of intense research and debate (Bartram et al., 2018 ; EU, 2019b ; Griggs et al., 2017 ; Priyadarshini and Abhilash, 2018 ; Yingchun et al., 2018 ). Science and policy can significantly contribute by acknowledging interconnections, evidence-based procedures and well-designed indicators, (Schmalzbauer and Visbeck, 2016 ). Success in achieving the Agenda will rely on the level of local commitment and enactment, integration of all SD dimensions and alignment with extant initiatives (Biermann et al., 2017 ). Localization  emerges as a relevant issue in the literature. Jones and Comfort (2019)  discuss localization challenges in the UK, concluding that lack of consistent policies, limited awareness, and funding gaps persists. Engström et al. (2019)  study cross-scale water, land, climate and energy interactions in Sweden. Terama et al. (2019)  explore the relation between urban sustainability and the SDGs with a Nordic perspective, claiming that is disconnected from local practice, demanding better integration and multidisciplinary approaches. Allen et al. (2019a)  explore nesting national models in Australia and claim for coherent policies and investments while acknowledging potential risks. Fukuda-Parr and Muchhala (2020)  analyse the role of developing countries, particularly the “agency of the South”, in shaping global norms. Schmieg et al. (2018)  argue that individual ethical norms at the micro-level could decelerate unsustainability at meso-level, while norms at the macro-level, such as the SGDs, may accelerate innovations. Kabisch et al. (2019)  underline the relevance of local contexts to achieve urban transformations. Schmidt-Traub et al. (2019)  suggest involving civil organisations and cities as key players towards localization the SDGs while enhancing integration into regulatory frameworks. Almost five years from the SDGs adoption, numerous Voluntary National Reviews are available (UN, 2019h ). However, most countries have not yet reported status (Allen et al., 2019a ) and VNRs have been dedicated to readiness and progress, rather than comparatives between policies or contexts, which remain underreported (Elder and Bartalini, 2019 ). Quantitative assessment of SDGs performance across counties is required (Allen et al., 2016 ; Engström et al., 2019 ; Huan et al., 2019 ; Lafortune and Schmidt-Traub, 2019 ; Salvia et al., 2019 ). Dickens et al. (2019)  plea for benchmarks and national indicators to avoid reducing significance. Complexities regarding different scales should be soundly considered by reconciling all specificities from the global to the local domains. Relevant methodological hurdles and data gaps and lags remain (Dang and Serajuddin, 2020 ; Guo, 2020 ; Jacob, 2017 ), hampering the effectiveness assessment. Hence, further research is desirable concerning measurement efforts (Elder and Bartalini, 2019 ). Integration in governance  based on science-based foundations, policy coherence, together with science-policy interfaces is another a central issue for the Agenda (EU, 2019b ; Georgeson and Maslin, 2018 ; Karlsson-Vinkhuyzen et al., 2018 ; Miola et al., 2019 ; OECD, 2019a ; OECD, 2019c ; Tosun and Leininger, 2017 ; UN, 2018b ; Valencia et al., 2019 ). Indeed, target 17.14 is specifically focused on policy coherence. However, current governance fragmentation could hinder the SDGs achievement (Monkelbaan, 2019 ). As widely acknowledged, the Agenda needs to move beyond monitoring and focusing on achieving a better balance among dimensions, leading to a systemic, holistic system-approach (Bartram et al., 2018 ; Coopman et al., 2016 ; Cutter, 2015 ; Liu et al., 2015 ; Miola et al., 2019 ; Zhou and Moinuddin, 2017 ; Stafford-Smith et al., 2017 ). Allen et al. (2018)  review the SDGs implementation in 26 countries, concluding that, despite progress, critical gaps prevail regarding interactions, showing prevalence of silo-based approaches, counterproductive for unleashing the transformative power of the Agenda. Dalby et al. (2019) , Stevens and Kanie (2016)  and Obersteiner et al. (2016)  argue that the Agenda requires an unprecedented integration effort to shift beyond siloes. Bartram et al. (2018)  critique the means of implementation of the SDG6 (water) providing policy recommendations to enhance this goal. Van der Hel and Biermann (2017)  compare six international science-based initiatives, related to SDGs governance, analyzing how they achieve the necessary “salience, credibility and legitimacy” to be soundly integrated within policy. Tosun and Leininger (2017)  argue that domestic policies strongly influence the implementation strategies, leading to divergent interpretations of interlinkages and integration. Indeed, “whole-of-government” and “whole-of-society” visions are needed to foster ownership and trigger governance transformations (OECD, 2019b ; UN, 2018b ). The SDGs should integrate both social and ecological well-being at all levels, but current integration and vertical diffusion remain poorly developed (Kanie et al., 2014 ). Indeed, Integration should consider three dimensions: horizontal, vertical, and engagement of stakeholders (UN, 2018b ; Valencia et al., 2019 ), including governance frameworks across geographies and scales to better deal with transboundary issues (Amos and Lydgate, 2019 ; OECD, 2019c ; Schmidt et al., 2017 ). The Agenda is indivisible, therefore unsuitable prioritization practices (i.e. “cherry picking”) could danger the holistic vision (Allen et al., 2018 ; Georgeson and Maslin, 2018 ; Zhou and Moinuddin, 2017 ). Manifest overlaps and uneven support are evidenced, together with poor coordination or accountability (ECOSOC, 2017 ). Certainly, accountability is feeble considered (Zimm et al., 2018 ), since proper tools are lacking and the Agenda mainly refers to monitoring instead. Bowen et al. (2017)  identify three governance challenges: raising collective action, equitably manage trade-offs, and providing mechanisms to secure accountability. Grainger-Brown and Malekpour (2019)  conclude that existing implementation methods lack transformative power and strategic acumen. Monkelbaan (2019)  suggest an “Integrative Sustainability Governance framework” standing upon power, knowledge and norms pillars and considering resilience, systemic discussions and behavior issues. Sullivan et al. (2018) , Caiado et al. (2018)  claim for innovative tools and analytical frameworks to overcome challenges. Kanie et al. (2019)  plea for action coherence and compliance, spurring transformative shifts both on science and policy sides while adapting extant instruments and policies. Reynolds et al. (2018)  explore the role of system-thinking towards pursuing the SDG17 (means of implementation), concluding that capacity building and action-learning platforms are required. Knowledge gaps persist, particularly amid social and ecological systems at local level, limiting the effectiveness of governance (Mastrángelo et al., 2019 ). Indeed, knowledge management is a relevant, yet overlooked, element which could support implementation (Barrantes-Briceño and Almada-Santos, 2019 ), enhancing policy and practice. Certainly, the literature reflects the need of a paradigm shift based on multi-level governance  and full stakeholders’ engagement (Dalby et al., 2019 ; EU, 2019b ; Martens, 2019 ; Messerli et al., 2019 ; UN, 2019d ). Caiado et al. (2018) , Giannetti et al. (2018)  and Horan (2019)  underscore the potential offered by partnerships and the need to reinforcing bottom-up approaches. However, this transformation is not straightforward, since multiple uncertainties remain regarding human-environment complexities, unknown cause-effect relations, conflicting views from different stakeholders and context-based specificities (Messerli et al., 2019 ). Thus, agents of change, actionable strategies and enhanced gears are needed to achieve the Goals (OECD, 2019c ; UN, 2019d ). Sachs et al. (2019b)  and the “The World in 2050 Initiative “(IIASA, 2019 ) concur that six shifts are required to spur progress, by tackling education and inequalities, health and well-being, low-carbon sustainable energy and industry, sustainable land use and food systems, smart cities and harnessing the digital revolution. Chapman and Shigetomi (2018)  recognize the relevance of the lifestyle factor. Fostering public involvement is crucial (EU, 2019b ; Li et al., 2018 ), but policy reforms are not sufficient, production and consumption patterns and power structures should be also transformed prioritizing human rights and planetary boundaries (Bengtsson et al., 2018 ; Martens, 2018 ). A narrative of change and disruptive transformation of the idea of development is unavoidable (Bengtsson et al., 2018 ; Stevens and Kanie, 2016 ; UN, 2019d ) by decoupling economic growth from environmental degradation shifting to circular economies (Schroeder et al., 2019 ). Policy and financial enabling frameworks are key critical leverage points (Dalby et al., 2019 ; Lagoarde-Segot, 2020 ; UN, 2019g ) yet poorly developed. Geopolitical stability and good governance have to be enhanced (IMF, 2019 ), together with incentives, investment label (UN, 2019d ), fostering international cooperation (UN, 2019g ) and promoting and debt and tax reforms (ECOSOC, 2019b ). In conclusion, multiple limitations exist to overcoming implementation challenges by achieving a truly whole-of-government integrative and context-savvy approach, able to engage all the stakeholders, avoiding counterproductive prioritization. Better coordination and accountability mechanisms guided by analytical frameworks, science-based approaches, sound policy enablers and a shift towards sustainable patterns are open issues yet to be decoded.  4.2 Following-up progress and assessing impact The SDGs realization strongly depends on monitoring and evaluation (Lu et al., 2015 ; OECD, 2019b ; UN, 2018b ; Schmidt-Traub et al., 2017 ), which indeed, relies on ensuring robust accountability (Bebbington and Unerman, 2018 ; Wackernagel et al., 2017 ). Measuring progress is an imperative for the implementation (Dalby et al., 2019 ; Persson et al., 2016 ; Reyers et al., 2017 ; Yonehara et al., 2017 ). However, current data vacuum, non-consistent indicators, limitations regarding data processing, interoperability and interpretation, together with absence of sound methodologies and coordinated efforts to compare progress across geographies (Crespo-Cuaresma et al., 2018 ) are hindering follow-up (Lior et al., 2018 ; Lu et al., 2015 ). Extant analytical and modeling tools are not able to cover the 169 targets, evidencing knowledge gaps and the need to reduce complexity and enhance accountability (Zimm et al., 2018 ). A taxonomy and systematic approaches for quantifying progress are absent (Xu et al., 2020 ). Besides, present emphasis on using institutional indicators is jeopardizing the understanding of effectiveness and overlooking interactions (Benson et al., 2019 ). Defining the measurement purpose in line with materiality assessment, together with proper integration within governance, are essential factors (Schönherr et al., 2019 ). First preliminary attempts to assess the SDGs framework have been undertaken, although the Agenda remains a voluntary effort, lacking mandatory reporting mechanisms. Monitoring efforts to date are mainly devoted to single indicators (Barbier and Burgess, 2019 ). An additional difficulty comes from the complex nature and interpretation of the SDGs, requiring further refinement and clarification of their underlying objectives (Dang and Serajuddin, 2020 ). Therefore, monitoring and evaluation should go beyond indicators and outcomes, by considering contextual issues (Fukuda-Parr and McNeill, 2019 ; Renn et al., 2020 ). Thus, ensuring that progress is relevant, equitable and truly sustainable (Schwandt et al., 2016 ). Indicators are not able to explain failures, wicked issues or complex dynamics, evidencing that a system-approach is needed (Renn et al., 2020 ). Localization is critical to overcome mismatches between theory-driven indicators and local conditions. The compatibility between accountability regimes and integrated policy approaches is controversial. Therefore, accountability should be reflected by using a context-based approach and shared governance among all actors, avoiding strong regimes (Karlsson-Vinkhuyzen et al., 2018 ). The definition of essential variables with systemic perspective is crucial, enabling both sustainable transformation and interaction at policy level (Reyers et al., 2017 ). Tracking the spatio-temporal dynamics of progress at global and local level seems an imperative (Xu et al., 2020 ). Even though, concepts such as sustainable value creation (Arnold, 2018 ; Dressler and Bucher, 2018 ), inclusivity or resilience, are especially difficult to assess (Bamberger et al., 2016 ; Yonehara et al., 2017 ). An inclusive approach is crucial to improve measurement, along with a proper narrative and understanding of priorities (Bidarbakhtnia, 2020 ). Multi-sectoral action and prevention-driven policies are required (Lozano et al., 2018 ). In summary, suitable context-based and systemic methodologies, accountability compatible with policy integration, data availability and granularity, along with and capacity building are critical elements to consider for evaluating progress, thus far untapped, requiring further efforts.  4.3 The role of performing indicators and data availability The SDGs indicators were adopted by 2017, after a tough process lead by the UN Statistics Division, wanting a scientific debate and influenced by politics struggles and competing interest (Kapto, 2019 ), hence resulting in relevant shortcomings (Brussel et al., 2019 ). Indeed, indicators are still being reviewed. As of March 2020, the classification contains 116 Tier-I indicators (those with well-built methodology and available data), 92 Tier-II indicators (lacking data coverage), 20 Tier-III (featured by both limited data and methodologies) and 4 with multiple-tiers (UN Statistics Division, 2020 ). Thus, a large number of SDG indicators lack data and agreed methodology, and some metrics could be contradictory (Bissio, 2019 ; Dawes, 2019 ; Hickel, 2019 ). Nevertheless, measuring SD is highly complex due to the underlying limitations of interpretation, multidimensional nature and tensions among dimensions, particularly regarding public goods. Monitoring requires a broad analysis of the goals essence. Therefore, indicators should be selected based on their ability to reflect the respective SDG value and not only on data availability. Further effort in enhancing Tier II and III indicators is highly necessary (Fukuda-Parr and McNeill, 2019 ). A relevant body of literature on SDGs indicators can be found, concluding that extant metrics suffer relevant limitations (Annan-Diab and Molinari, 2017 ; Barbier and Burgess, 2019 ; Dlouhá and Pospíšilová, 2018 ; Galli et al., 2018 ; Hák et al., 2016 ; Lehtonen et al., 2016 ; Salvia et al., 2019 ; Storey et al., 2017 ; UN, 2019c ; World Bank, 2019 ). Current framework has been criticized for being narrow, inflexible and unfeasible; hence undermining the purpose of monitoring what matters instead of doing what can be measured with official statistics (Georgeson and Maslin, 2018 ). Indicators can be considered reductionist both based on its conception and use (Ordaz, 2019 ), and assessment based on present metrics could be confusing (Janoušková et al., 2018 ). Mapping indicators could be useful to devise solutions, while avoiding threats (Kraak et al., 2018 ). Measurement challenges are linked to interpretation, demanding a refinement of the goal formulation and subtle purposes, as well as cost-effective solutions to bridge the data vacuum (Dang and Serajuddin, 2020 ). Certainly, relevant data gaps and lags remain (Jacob, 2017 ), as well as underreported transboundary impacts such us migration (UN-SDSN, 2019 ). Thus far, data is available for half of the indicators (Dang and Serajuddin, 2020 ) and only 50 of the 169 SDG targets are ready for progress assessment (Martens, 2018 ). Most uncertainties and challenging measures are linked to the SDG12 (Dalberg, 2017 ) and those goals related to ecosystems and climate (i.e. SDG13, 14, 15), (OECD, 2019c ). Indeed data gaps affect 77% of environmental-related goals (UNEP, 2019 ). Good progress has been made only on 23% of the environment-focused indicators owing to a combination of policy, reporting and funding. The majority suffers negative trends, therefore further data and actions are needed to accelerate progress (UNEP, 2019 ). Lozano et al. (2018)  and Hall et al. (2019)  underscore uncertainties and data limitation concerning the Health-related goal (SDG3), which could yields inaccurate aggregation, estimation and modelling. Trade-offs and spill-overs across boundaries have been ignored by most metrics, therefore they should be considered (Schmidt-Traub et al., 2019 ; Sachs et al., 2017 ). Better data collection, analysis and interpretation are looked-for (Lozano et al., 2018 ), not only to measure progress but also to make corrections and projections (UN-SDSN, 2019 ). Regarding the SDG11 on cities, inconsistencies are uncovered in relation to other frameworks, revealing the need of comprehensive approaches to benchmarking and assessing policies (Giles-Corti et al., 2019 ). Ulbrich et al. (2019)  explore how inequalities could affect the representativeness of the SDG indicators, particularly on the goal devoted to cities (SDG11). Dickens et al. (2019)  suggest defining tangible targets at national level, along with indicators and benchmarks to avoid reducing the significance of prescribed global indicators, while allowing comparative evaluation. In absence of representative and integrated measures, persistent bias would remain. Most experts claim for a critical review of current indicators, alongside the formulation of alternative metrics and benchmarks going beyond the “obsolete” GDP indicator (Costanza et al., 2104 ; Hák et al., 2016 ; Mair et al., 2018 ; Merry, 2019 ; Rubio-Mozos et al., 2019 ; Saito et al., 2017 ; Schmidt-Traub et al., 2017 ; Suciu and Năsulea, 2019 ). GDP indicators (i.e. SDG8) contradict the principles of sustainability efforts, since strongly clashes with those goals devoted to ecology and equality (Coscieme et al., 2020 ). Current frames underachieve on sustainability, yielding cascade effects on poverty and inequality (Wackernagel et al., 2017 ). Vanham et al. (2019)  analyse environmental footprints overlaps with indicators and explore their potential to assess progress towards the SDGs and the “water-energy-food-ecosystem” nexus. Fioramonti et al. (2019) , Ivanov and Peleah (2018)  and Suciu and Năsulea. (2019)  plea for alternative indicators for wellbeing, to reinforce the sustainability narrative by connecting a new economic paradigm compatible with the goals (i.e. Happy Planet Index, Sustainable Human Development Index). For instance, the “Inclusive Wealth Index” (UNEP, 2018 ) is a simple index, comprising the natural, human and manufactured capital which could complement the GDP, even though it represents a weak sustainability concept. Composited multi-dimensional indexes going beyond national implementation could help to overcome some limitations (Diaz-Sarachaga et al., 2018 ; Campagnolo et al., 2018 ; Lozano et al., 2018 ), and offering a holistic approach (Lior et al., 2018 ). Lemke and Bastini (2020)  suggest a “Multilevel Sustainable Development Index (MLSDI)” able to overcome limitations of existing indices, which hardly reflect all the dimensions of sustainability and are unable to compare agents at different levels due to methodological caveats. In the corporate realm, harmonized indicators and methodologies for reporting are critical, avoiding narrow approaches (GRI-UN Global Compact, 2018 ). Nevertheless, novel metrics and composited indexes suffer limitations and should be enhanced, including potential contrasting effects (Bondarchik et al., 2016 ). Hence, crafting novel science-based metrics, together with context-friendly measures, leveraging new data sources, better aggregation and integration, are urgently needed in order to ensure that, despite the challenges, all targets can be measured, all countries can monitor progress and transboundary issues can be considered.  4.4 Adoption and progress as of 2019 Thus far, progress has been evidenced, although is slow-paced and unevenly distributed across contexts and actors (Sachs et al., 2019a ; UN, 2019a ; UN, 2019b ). Hurdles to wide-ranging achievement persist (Bryan et al., 2019 ). The world is off-target, since no country has yet achieved the goals (Dalby et al., 2019 ; OECD, 2019c ) and even top-performing countries face challenges (Sachs et al., 2019a ). The first SDG-Summit held in September 2019 (UN, 2019f ) pleaded for a decade of urgent action. Current policies and frames yield limited progress, therefore deep and urgent transformations are required to accelerate progress (Moyer and Hedden, 2020 ). Political leadership and engagement from all stakeholders are urgently needed to increase funds and spur action to catalyze change (UN, 2019d ). Key cornerstones for tacking stock on progress are, inter-alia: the annual report from United Nations “Progress Report towards the SDGs” providing a perspective of the goals status; the SDG Index and Dashboards (UN-SDSN, 2019 ) which classify countries according to their performance, however, it has been criticized for its limited consistency and low performance on sustainability (Wackernagel et al., 2017 ); the SDG Atlas (World Bank, 2018a ) which debates measuring methods for Tier-3 indicators. Other relevant documents are “Measuring Distance to the SDG Targets 2019” (OECD, 2019c ) concerning the OECD countries and the Eurostat Monitoring Report, describing progress within the EU (EU, 2019a ). According to these reports, social inclusion and environmental-related goals are alarmingly worsening, showing a worrisome biodiversity loss, exacerbation of the climate crisis, unsustainable pollution of oceans, consumption and rising footprints (ADB, 2019 ; Sachs et al., 2019a ; UNEP, 2019 ). Actually, projected extreme poverty and hungry indicators are unbearable and access to health services remains deficient. Inequalities and conflicts are rising and humanitarian crisis are enduring, placing human rights in danger in many contexts, threatening social cohesion and progress (UN HLPF, 2019 ). Climate action is perceived as an urgent priority (Dalby et al., 2019 ), followed by responsible consumption and production (Globescan-Sustainability, 2019 ). Besides, transboundary issues, such as migration, and ignored spill-overs are hindering progress in vital goals (Sachs et al., 2017 ; Sachs et al., 2019a ; Schmidt-Traub et al., 2019 ; UN, 2019b ). Moreover, some megatrends might be threatening the Goals: the unsustainable growth model, weakened multilateralism overlooking transboundary responsibilities, strong structural and financial barriers such as debt crisis, unfair trade, and democracy loss worldwide. Existing reports show different, occasionally contradictory, conclusions depending on the approach used, evidencing a fragmented knowledge regarding progress, extremely context-dependent. Methodologies and tools are unsatisfactory and often inconsistent, lacking an agreed systematized measure of progress (Xu et al., 2020 ). Thus, new approaches tackling the context, offering strategic directions and fostering commitment and collaboration are required (ADB, 2019 ). Sabau (2020)  argues that present lack of progress relies on a narrow understanding about sustainability values and claims to reorient governance towards a “tetravaluation” approach across key actors, based on: “exchange, labor, use and function value”. Moyer and Hedden (2020)  propose an integrated assessment model (International Futures) to evaluate progress toward target nine indicators related to human development. Biermann et al. (2017)  underline the relevance of aligning the SDGs with extant initiatives, integrating all SD dimensions. Lozano et al. (2018)  highlight the need of multi-sectoral common action and prevention-driven policy action. Therefore, poor progress to date results of a number of hurdles which need to be tackled, in terms of institutional coordination and capacity, adoption and ownership, flawed assessment standards, together with unsuitable indicators, methodologies and data gaps. New metrics, specific tools and tailored guidelines for accountability and reporting, by harnessing technology and new data sources, are required to cover the SDGs singularities.  4.5 Roles, responsibilities and collaboration among actors Achieving the SDGs will require close-knit collaboration, cooperation and coordination efforts, as well as shared responsibility from all stakeholders, to move beyond intentions into actions (Frey and Sabbatino, 2018 ; Kurz, 2020 ) (Fig. 4  ). In this regard, ensuring policy coherence, improving legitimacy (Donoher, 2017 ) and active engagement of business, governments and civil society is crucial (Galli et al., 2018 ; Lucas et al., 2016 ; Messerli et al., 2019 ; Schönherr et al., 2017 ; UN, 2019d ). However, the roles within the Agenda have not been properly discussed nor soundly established (Spangenberg, 2017 ). Responsibilities should be defined to mediate with trade-offs and spill-overs, as well as to unravel the potential contribution from all actors. Given the ambition of the SDGs, promoting collaboration and activating narratives of change, are essential factors for overcoming hurdles and inertia, towards accelerating progress (Dalby et al., 2019 ; UN, 2019d ). Business realm The private sector plays a decisive role in the implementation and financing of the Agenda (Cordova and Celone, 2019 ; Frey and Sabbatino, 2018 ; Kolk et al., 2017 ; Priyadarshini and Abhilash, 2018 ; Van der Waal and Thijssens, 2020 ; Van Zanten and Van Tulder, 2018 ). Companies are expected to lead the transformation, by motivating market sustainability, crafting sustainable approaches (CSIL, 2017 ), building purpose-driven culture and inspiring their supply chains (Deloitte, 2017 ; UN Global Compact-Accenture, 2019 ). Mainstreaming the SDGs is not straightforward (Elder and Bartalini, 2019 ), since open issues and lack of understanding about added-value and actionable strategies remain (Betti et al., 2018 ; Van Zanten and Van Tulder, 2018 ). Unsuitable means of implementation, together with institutional or cultural factors could hinder adoption and progress (Rosati and Faria, 2019 ). Van der Waal and Thijssens (2020)  study the motivations that could guide companies to get actively involved within the Agenda, to create shared value for “common good”, concluding that business involvement is limited and “evasive”, apparently encouraged by “legitimacy” and institutional factors. Indeed, businesses are gradually committed with the Agenda, and progress is being made, supported by tools like the SDG Compass (WBCSD, 2015 ), CEO Guide (WBCSD, 2017 ), Good Life Goals (WBCSD, 2019c ), SDG Matrix (UN Global Compact-KPMG, 2017 ) or the Blueprint for Business (UN Global Compact, 2017 ). However, companies are not taking appropriate accountability actions, nor sufficiently aligning materiality with the SDGs (KPMG, 2018 ; Martinuzzi et al., 2017 ; WBSCD, 2019b ). SDG adoption is moving ahead, with the SDG13 (climate) on top and ecological goals on bottom. Nevertheless, corporate involvement persists limited and obscure in terms of governance, as well as highly dependent of the type of SDG (Vazquez-Brust et al., 2020 ). A controversial discussion relies on how to prioritize actions based on principles (UN Global Compact-Accenture, 2019 ), while avoiding “cherry-picking” counterproductive decisions (CSIL, 2017 ), which would threat the holistic mission of the Agenda. It seems that companies are prioritizing some SDGs focused on climate, well-being or growth at the expenses of the ecosystems or food security (KPMG, 2018 ). Another notorious aspect to avoid is the use of the SDGs as “green-washing” marketing instrument to induce behavior change in consumers or stakeholders, which could yield to a loss of trust (Pimonenko et al., 2020 ). Rendtorff (2019)  analyses the SDGs role as a governance tool and driver for business ethics and CSR, triggering transformation of the economic model. Martinuzzi and Schönherr (2019)  claim for impact-oriented, collaborative, data-intensive, universal systemic framework to guide companies in better measuring their impacts. Cordova and Celone (2019)  underline the relevant role of industrial innovation as a driver of the SDGs. Govindan et al. (2020)  study the particular barriers of the SDGs in relation to the novel “industrial sharing economy” paradigm. Van Tulder (2018)  advises purpose-driven business to tackle wicked problems, guided by principles to understand and integrate the Goals. Redman (2018)  claims for addressing communication, tactical and strategic issues. Vildåsen (2018)  perceives the SDGs as a platform for debating social and environmental concerns with stakeholders, facilitating collaboration. Rubio-Mozos et al. (2019)  underline the role of purpose-driven SMEs and entrepreneurs claiming for a new systemic “engagement ecosystem” shifting to a sustainable growth model aligned with the Agenda. A framework for SDG business reporting has been designed by leveraging the Global Reporting Initiative (GRI) standards, the Global Compact and the Responsible Investment principles (GRI-UN Global Compact, 2018 ) although it presents limitations. Szennay et al. (2019)  analyses the relation among the SDGs and GRI indicators confirming a strong link but also business coverage gaps. Muff et al. (2017)  suggest a framework to translate goals into business opportunities, nurturing the SDG Compass which presents ambiguous and low-ambitious scope. Current tools are not appropriate, evidencing lack of proper indicators (Lopez, 2020 ), open-sources rankings or standards based on impact. Hence better guides and standards are urgently required (Topple et al., 2017 ). In conclusion, embedding the SDG into the corporate strategy and reporting, while avoiding counterproductive prioritization is essential.
