This relies on fostering collaboration with stakeholders and building novel guidelines and tools towards raising ownership. Regardless the utmost relevance of business for achieving a sustainable future, little is known about how companies can integrate the goals into corporate governance and business models (Dahlmann et al., 2019 ) demanding further and inter-disciplinary research (Dalby et al., 2019 ). Academic realm.  The relevance of science, research and education communities for catalyzing the SDGs has been broadly acknowledged (Colglazier, 2015 ; ECOSOC, 2019a ; Leal-Filho et al., 2019b ; Kopnina, 2020 ; Messerli et al., 2019 ; Priyadarshini and Abhilash, 2018 ; Saito et al., 2017 ; Schmalzbauer and Visbeck, 2016 ; Stafford-Smith et al., 2018 ; UN, 2019d ). Undeniably, science is crucial to provide significant indicators and monitoring systems to understand interactions and complexities (Schmalzbauer and Visbeck, 2016 ; UN, 2019d ). Nonetheless, profound understanding of its contribution, along with proper indicators, roadmaps and reporting processes are required (Colglazier, 2015 ). Education, is a multiplier for sustainability, therefore, new transformational educative models (Zamora-Polo and Sánchez-Martín, 2019 ) and governance frameworks (Kickbusch and Hanefeld, 2017 ) which activate sustainability are required, although it remains a challenge (Giangrande et al., 2019 ). Current education role within the Agenda is questioned, for being unable to recognize extant paradoxes, favoring an economic-based notion of development, lacking ecology ethics (Kopnina, 2020 ). Despite progress, a lack of capacity persists in most academic institutions regarding sustainable practices. Gaps in research and practice particularly in terms of demographic diversity could hinder education for sustainability (Rashid, 2019 ). Moreover, science and research communities should yet agree a common ground about ethical and epistemic values around sustainability (Schneider et al., 2019 ). Public Sector and Governments.  Given the enabling character of public institutions they play a leading role driving the Agenda governance (UN , 2018b ; UN, 2019b ; UN, 2019e ), particularly towards supporting the SDG16. A holistic approach comprising all the dimensions of sustainability and related interlinks, together with the reinforcement of synergies, alignment and collaboration across scales is required in order to breakdown silos (Cutter, 2015 ; Dalby et al., 2019 ). New integrative systemic models and tools are needed to guide policy-making (Collste et al., 2017 ; Liu et al., 2015 ). Civil Society.  Little attention has been paid to the role of civil society (Martens, 2019 ) and individuals, which is also linked to localization, despite their huge potential contribution to the SDGs (Li et al., 2018 ). Indeed, consumption represents the 60% of the global wealth (UNCTAD, 2019 ), thus, citizens are relevant agents in pursuing a more sustainable society (Micheletti et al., 2014 ). The historic global demonstrations for climate action on 2019, have shown the potential behind mobilizing civil society towards sustainability purposes. However, spaces for civil society participation are limited, therefore constraining the respective influence on sustainable policy and the SDGs implementation, revealing a “democracy-influence paradox” (Sénit, 2019 ). Citizen science also constitutes a relevant data source which could be integrated into the SDGs reporting system (Fritz et al., 2019 ). Public awareness, citizen engagement, ownership and people-centric indicators are critical and must be enhanced (EESC, 2018 ; Guan et al., 2019 ; Lucci and Lucci, 2015 ; Rubio-Mozos et al., 2019 ; UN, 2019d ; UNU-CS, 2018 ) and bottom-up governance should be better leveraged (Martens, 2019 ). Knowledge about behavior formation towards the goals is limited (Guan et al., 2019 ). Hence, further research and policy development is desirable to fully explore the meaningful contribution of civil society to change patterns and accelerating change. Multi-stakeholder partnerships.  These alliances are fundamental drivers of SD, helping to rally different actors while bridging market failures, free-riders issues or governance gaps (Nelson, 2017 ; Van Zanten and Van Tulder, 2018 ). Thus, cross-sectoral, public-private and collaborative partnerships are considered crucial elements for successfully achieving and funding the Agenda in line with the UN Addis-Abeba Action Agenda (UN, 2015c ; Dalby et al., 2019 ; Florini and Pauli, 2018 ; Horan, 2019 ; Kolk et al., 2017 ; OECD, 2019c ; UN, 2019d ; UN-ECOSOC, 2019b ; UN-SDSN, 2019 ). Certainly, the 17.16 and 17.17 targets focus on effective public-private and civil society partnerships. Particularly, data partnerships are essential for a responsible use of digital technologies (Data4SDGS, 2019 ; Data-Pop Alliance, 2017 ; UN-SDSN, 2019 ; WEF, 2019d ). Rasche et al. (2019)  argue that enhanced legitimacy and accountability are needed to evolve the use of data for development through partnerships. Susha (2020)  discusses that data collaborations are promising although little is known about how to successfully implement such partnerships. However, articulating partnerships and bringing together different stakeholders is not forthright. Strong complexities, implementation and governance hurdles, unclear impacts (Berrone et al., 2019 ; UN DESA, 2019 ) or limited effectiveness persist (Beisheim and Ellersiek, 2017 ; Melo, 2018 ; Nelson, 2017 ). Horan (2019)  reveals two main caveats regarding identification and implementation of transformative and efficient partnerships, and suggests enhancing: “compensation, collaboration, capacity, coordination and incentives”. Success strongly depends upon stable political and economic structures not always available. Aligning actors, providing incentives and instruments are also challenges. The added-value brought by the partnerships has yet to be clarified and they need to be scale-up to leverage their transformative power (UN Global Compact -Accenture, 2018 ). Further research based on facts and contextual understanding is needed for leapfrogging and scaling the partnerships for the SDGs. In conclusion, clarifying the roles, responsibilities and unlocking the engagement, ownership and collaboration of all the actors, at all levels of society in the same direction, is essential to accelerate transformation and progress for pursuing the goals.   4.6 The role of technology, innovation diffusion and knowledge transfer Science, Technology and Innovation (STI) are key requirements towards SD (EC, 2015 ; Köhler et al., 2019 ; Perez, 2019 ; Sachs et al., 2019a ; Vatananan-Thesenvitz et al., 2019 ) and should be central for the SDGs implementation (Colglazier, 2015 ; Walsh et al., 2020 ). A relevant literature about the relation between STI and sustainable transitions exists, underlining the need of novel paradigms (Köhler et al., 2019 ). The role of STI in enabling a carbon-neutrality (Labanca et al., 2020 ) and improving efficiency and effectiveness of SD (Berawi, 2017 ) is widely acknowledged. Furthermore, eco-innovation is considered a driver for the SDGs (Cordova and Celone, 2019 ). Indeed, the Agenda and the Paris Climate Agreement rely on the diffusion of green innovative technologies (Centobelli et al., 2020 ; Walz et al., 2017 ). However, sustainability issues cannot be solved just by technology (Kane et al., 2019 ; Söderholm et al., 2019 ; Secundo et al., 2020 ). Walsh et al. (2020)  denounce a narrow consideration of STI in framing the SDGs through the UN Technology Facilitation Mechanism (UN, 2018a ), since is featured by a disproportionate focus on technology transfer and little on eco-innovation, wanting planetary boundary awareness (Imaz and Sheinbaum, 2017 ). Moreover, social innovation is poorly understood and considered (Eichler and Schwarz, 2019 ). Knowledge gaps among social and ecological systems remain (Mastrángelo et al., 2019 ), together with tensions between technological progress and environmental conservation. The SDG12 (target 12.a) and SDG17 (17.7.1) support, respectively, technology capacity and transfer in developing countries, as well as diffusion of “environmentally sound technologies”, but without setting truly actionable objectives (Gasper et al., 2019 ) and lacking measurement methodologies. Thus, they are unable to overcome market-induced tensions which limit diffusion of green technologies such as Intelectual Property Rights (IPR). Furthermore, access to technology depends on multiple dynamics and environmental performance is context-dependent. Thus, literature evidences that policy, along with reinforced means of implementation and finance, are crucial elements to mediate with those tensions (Sinha et al., 2020 ; Walsh et al., 2020 ). The diffusion and integration of the SDGs within the heart of STI policy is essential (Walsh et al., 2020 ). STI narratives should be enhanced by knowledge-sharing and collaboration across geographies and actors (Berawi, 2017 ), promoting co-creation (Stafford-Smith et al., 2017 ). Indeed, a knowledge-based society is an imperative for realizing the Agenda (Colglazier, 2015 ), since knowledge plays a vital role in the international development realm by bridging local and global insights amidst research, policy and practice. The goals act like vehicles that arrange and disseminate norms, value creation and policy improvement (Fukuda-Parr and McNeill, 2019 ), constituting a relevant frame to knowledge brokering among societal actors (Cummings et al., 2019 ). However, literature pays little attention to this subject (Barrantes-Briceño and Almada-Santos, 2019 ). The intellectual capital and creativity act as enablers for transitions towards an innovative, knowledge-based, smart and sustainable society (Herman and Suciu, 2019 ; Secundo et al., 2020 ; Suciu and Năsulea, 2019 ). Moreover, the interlink amidst human, structural and relational capital is essential to create societal value (Secundo et al., 2020 ), although rests little explored (Gouvea et al., 2018 ; Secundo et al., 2020 ), evidencing inclusiveness gaps. Policy diffusion of ecologically-aware innovation and green technologies is a critical tool for cleaner production and SD (Crespi et al., 2015 ; Centobelli et al., 2020 ), since it facilitates knowledge spill-overs (Aldieri et al., 2019 ) leading to a virtuous cycle of eco-innovation. Thus, policies should promote knowledge transfer between different sources for allowing eco-innovation and SD (Aldieri et al., 2020 ). However, derived negative externalities, “free-riding” incentives and potential tensions amid genuine sustainability and productivity maximization should be acknowledged by appropriate policy mix and knowledge absorption (Aldieri et al., 2019 ). Further research is required on breakthrough and diffusion Köhler et al. (2019) . The SDGs diffusion could collide at global scale due to persistent silos but could be possible close to the implementation level (Kanie et al., 2014 ). Top-down diffusion of innovations can be difficult in specific contexts, where informal networks can be suitable enablers for dissemination (Henry, 2018 ; Masuda et al., 2018 ). Indeed, innovation strategies are context-dependent (Eichler and Schwarz, 2019 ), while sharing common factors to succeed such as an “enabling environment, absorptive capacity, learning and diffusion”. Thus, policy should balance diffusion and absorptive capacity depending on the maturity of the innovation system (UNCTAD, 2017b ). Research on diffusion dynamics of sustainable innovation is thus far limited (Clausen and Fichter, 2016 ) particularly concerning bottom-up grassroots innovations (Feola and Butt, 2017 ). Patnaik and Bhowmick (2020)  underscore the relevance of fostering inclusive innovation and native knowledge for SD, overcoming extant asymmetries and ensuring ownership, transformative learning and psychological nuances. Hötte (2020)  contends that slow diffusion of green technologies could be partially caused by path dependence of technological change and cumulative knowledge. Pradhan et al. (2020)  argue that innovation diffusion and investments impact the economic growth in Europe, the direction of the causality is dependent of the specific measures utilized for that purpose. Current green growth and innovation perspectives are narrowly techno-centric, mainly focused on economic development, which overlook environmental concerns (Capasso et al., 2019 ) and neglect complexity, social and systemic interactions (Labanca et al., 2020 ). Social impacts of technological innovations and technology transfer have been disregarded in the literature (Corsi et al., 2020 ) and should be diffused to guarantee a genuine sustainability. Zhou et al. (2020)  explore the partial influence of fairness appreciation, embedders and knowledge-sharing in enhancing green innovation, as essential factor towards the SDGs. Schot and Steinmueller (2018)  suggests that ecosystems and socio-technical system change should be integrated, overcoming current lack of knowledge provision from the private sector. Building-up competences for tackling complexities and social issues seems crucial (Labanca et al., 2020 ), along with reorientation of technology considering market, structural and transformational failures at different scales (Capasso et al., 2019 ). Stimulating a Responsible Research and Innovation (RRI) approach to understand the complexities (Thapa et al., 2019 ; Van der Leeuw, 2018 ) along with facilitating inclusive innovation among low-income segments are priority facets which should be embedded in policies (Foster and Heeks, 2013 ; Hanlin and Kaplinsky, 2016 ; Zanello et al., 2013 ). However, the RRI concept and related policies persist ambiguous and should be well formulated considering: “inclusion, anticipation, responsiveness, reflexivity, as well as sustainability and care” (Burget et al., 2017 ). For instance, Responsible Innovation in Health (RIH) represents a promising approach for supporting numerous SDGs, demanding novel business models and social finance (Lehoux et al., 2018 ). Novel approaches, technologies and competences, including design thinking (Maher et al., 2018 ), and innovative business models (Arnold, 2018 ; Dressler and Bucher, 2018 ) are also desirable. Several authors devote research to the domain of digital technologies. Fukuda-Parr and McNeill (2019)  underscore the role of Big Data and digital technologies in shaping knowledge (epistemology) for SD and the SDGs in particular. Pappas et al. (2018)  argue that digitalization, specifically Big Data and analytic ecosystems, could speed up a transition to sustainability, provided that the interrelations amid actors are well understood, leading to value and knowledge creation. Stuermer et al. (2017)  underline that literature has considered digital artifacts as means towards SD, although they should comply with a number of prerequisites for sustainability. Tosic and Zivkovic (2019)  claim that a correlation between knowledge and innovation can be found, compatible with preserving sustainability approach. Knowledge-sharing policies in smart cities focused on merging green and smart concepts are an emerging, yet untapped, field (Israilidis et al., 2019 ). The WEF (2020a)  pleas for increasing the technology intensity of the SDGs strategic and implementation actions, aligning the Agenda with the 4th Industrial and Digital Revolution. Nevertheless, excessive “Technopolitics of Development” leaded by private philanthropists that use developing contexts as living labs for experimentation, could challenge participation and trigger undesirable consequences (Fejerskov, 2017 ). Reframing the STI policy agendas to promote and disseminating green technological innovations seems essential (Centobelli et al., 2020 ). Extant frames should be reoriented to encourage transformation towards SD (Walsh et al., 2020 ), based on context-savvy solutions (Centobelli et al., 2020 ), multi-scale and inclusive perspectives (Leach et al., 2012 ), novel technologies and principles, and a suitable mix of strategic actions, policy and finance (Rogge and Reichardt, 2016 ; Edmondson et al., 2018 ; Walsh et al., 2020 ). Hence, Technological innovation is crucial for achieving the goals, but it should be guided by a consistent policy and systemic governance able to overcome persistent barriers and market failures. A profound understanding of potential STI contribution is needed (Colglazier, 2015 ).Further understanding about the role, drivers and dynamics of technological innovation for SD and knowledge flows is needed, together with specific enabling regulation, effective and inclusive policies to bridge barriers while reinforcing social good (Vatananan-Thesenvitz et al., 2019 ).   5 The convergence amid sustainability and digitalization 5.1 A brief introduction to the powerful digital transformation The advent of digital paradigms, together with the explosion of computational power, have triggered a new era heralded to revolutionize science (Schmidt and Cohen, 2013 ) and facilitate a data-driven economy (WEF, 2019b ) and knowledge society (Grech and Camilleri, 2017 ). Digital technologies are disrupting across the economy and society. The data deluge, processed by intelligent algorithms, endows a huge connectivity and computational capacity, which combined with traditional methodologies and contextual nuances, can provide unprecedented capacities to explore, understand, predict, optimize and innovate aspects that were inscrutable before (Hey et al., 2009 ; Manyika, 2011 ; McKinsey, 2016 ). Digitalization brings novel cultural, holistic and multidimensional phenomena (Boyd and Crawford, 2012 ) beyond and across disciplines, blurring frontiers (Etzion and Aragon-Correa, 2016 ), and driving a complex and highly interconnected network (Letouzé and Pentland, 2018 ). The digital era cornerstones, offer huge potential to create new value propositions, enabling operational performance and efficiency, supporting wise decision-making and accountability, and building capacities or awareness. Given its exceptional power to approach complex societal issues, it has become an integral part of governmental agendas (Etzion and Aragon-Correa, 2016 ; UN, 2019b ; World Bank, 2018b ). Thus, the digital revolution grants a powerful tool to build-up smart green growth (Perez, 2019 ) and sustainable governance (Etzion and Aragon-Correa, 2016 ; Ki-Moon, 2014 ; Seele, 2016a ) which primarily relies on harnessing the so-called “data revolution for sustainable development” (Jacob, 2017 ; UN, 2014 ). However, digitalization is often surrounded hyperbolic expectations and unrealistic promises (Kiron and Unruh, 2018 ) bringing about multiple concerns and yet unknown risks (Letouzé and Pendtland, 2018 ). Its potential to spur SD is still controversial (Fernández-Portillo et al., 2019 ). Science and society are at a crossroad, being an upheaval or metamorphosis, where technology will transform the way we live and the whole society (Helbing et al., 2017 ).  5.2 The nexus between digitalization and sustainability Digitalisation1  1 Digitalization can be interpreted as the use of digital technologies to provide new value-producing opportunities (Gartner, 2020 ). In this article, digitalization can be perceived as the process by which economy and society are evolved around digital technologies providing new added-value opportunities.   and sustainability are game-changers and strategic imperatives which are converging, triggering major transformations (Kiron and Unruh, 2018 ; Osburg and Lohrmann, 2017 ). The convergence between digitalization and sustainability is perceived as a winning combination, not exempt of challenges, offering opportunities within and across organizational boundaries for overcoming informational void (Atos, 2018 ),. Other experts consider that both are conflicting concepts leading to a paradigm shift in social and ecological systems (Gebhardt, 2017 ). However, the nexus amid both domains remains poorly explored and little is known about whether and to what extent the digital era could contribute or jeopardize sustainable landscapes (Seele and Lock, 2017 ), what is the role of digital technologies in pursuing the Agenda and how to enable a sustainable digitalization. Therefore, a responsible and well-enunciated synergy between digitalization and sustainability is unavoidable and thus far untapped. The potential of ICT paradigms, particularly Big Data, to spur sustainability while enhancing transparency and accountability, has been widely recognized (Corbett, 2018 ; De Camargo-Fiorini et al., 2019 ; GeSI, 2019 ; Gijzen, 2013 ; Gouvea et al., 2018 ; Hilty and Aebischer, 2015 ; ITU, 2017b ; El Hilali and Manouar, 2020 ; Madudova et al., 2018 ; Seele, 2017 ; Seele and Lock, 2017 ; UN, 2019b ; WEF, 2020a ; World Bank, 2018b ; Zhao et al., 2017 ). The so-called “for good” movement does not only allude non-profit activities but also purpose-driven actions creating shared value. Nevertheless, a lack of consensus about the meaning of “social good” persist (Berendt, 2019 ). Digital paradigms are considered enablers of SD (Fernández-Portillo et al., 2019 ; Van der Velden, 2018a ), since they could support human progress, enhance personalization and reframe the business case for both investors and consumers (Holst et al., 2017 ), transforming behaviors and enabling circular economy (Atos, 2019 ). Besides, digitalization could enhance transparency, help to assess Environmental, Social and Governance (ESG) performance (Kiron and Unruh, 2018 ; Seele, 2016b ), drive resource efficiency, accelerate discovery (WEF, 2020a ), promote education (Ahel and Lingenau, 2020 ), support evidence-based decision-making and predictions (GeSI, 2019 ; ITU, 2017b ; Tjoa and Tjoa, 2016 ). However, strong controversies and uncertainties persist, particularly about the relationship between ICTs and sustainability at the corporate realm (Jones et al., 2017 ; Saunila et al., 2019 ; Ziemba, 2019 ). Digitalization may lead to unintended environmental or social effects (Scholz et al., 2018 ; Van der Velden, 2018a ), such as degradation of natural ecosystems (Bekaroo et al., 2016 ), amplification of power-asymmetries or digital divides (ITU, 2017b ). However, it also may help to reduce inequality (Asongu and Odhiambo, 2019 ) or financial access (Tchamyou et al., 2019 ), depending on how it is shaped. Digitalization could cause negative environmental and health impacts (LaPointe, 2018 ) linked to electronic waste (Aksin-Sivrikaya and Bhattacharya, 2017 ; WEF, 2019c ; WEF, 2020a ), raw materials production and disposal impacting biodiversity (Baldé et al., 2017 ; Khakurel et al., 2018 ; Osburg and Lohrmann, 2017 ; WEF , 2020a ) growing energy and water use, together with pollutant emissions (ITU, 2017b ; Tjoa and Tjoa, 2016 ). ICTs have been associated to awful effects on human rights related to minerals extraction (i.e. Coltan) in conflicting zones (Aksin-Sivrikaya and Bhattacharya, 2017 ). Bieser and Hilty (2018)  denounce a gap on indirect environmental effects of ICTs, which affect patterns of production or consumption, particularly consumer behavior. Thus, numerous aspects of ICTs could be intrinsically unsustainable (Unwin, 2017 ), remaining poorly studied (ITU, 2017b ; Letouzé and Pentland, 2018 ; MacFeely, 2019 ). Regarding energy consume and carbon emissions, digital technologies are “Janus-faced”, opening controversial debates (Schulte et al., 2016 ; Wang et al., 2015 ; Yan et al., 2018 ). ICTs are perceived as low-carbon enablers holding the potential to promote an efficient energy landscape (Bekaroo et al., 2016 ; Bull, 2015 ; Gouvea et al., 2018 ; Jacob, 2018 ; Mirza et al., 2019 ) but their massive development could yield a huge energy demand (Bekaroo et al., 2016 ; Strubell et al., 2019 ). Belkhir and Elmeligi (2018)  assess ICT footprint, concluding that the digital sector would be responsible of 14% of the green-house emissions, providing recommendations to curb them. Perils exist concerning growing negative footprints or perverse cascading effects increasing vulnerabilities due to the interconnected global system (Helbing, 2012 ). On the contrary, Jacob (2018)  explores the role of ICTs towards enabling a climate-friendly low carbon future, concluding that a net-positive impact is possible, provided that suitable policy, infrastructures and means are implemented. Other scholars (Gelenbe and Caseau, 2015 ; Moyer and Hughes, 2012 ) and studies (GeSI, 2016 ) underline the ICTs ability to reduce emissions, evidencing an “inverted-U” relation (Higón et al., 2017 ). Hence, despite the burgeoning literature, contradictory views, lack of understanding and poor accountability remain about the net impact, along with rebound and induction effects of digitalization (Gensch et al., 2017 ; Pohl et al., 2019 ). Thus, in-depth research and integrated management of ICTs and environment is desirable (Wang et al., 2015 ). Moreover, achieving the all-out net-sustainable benefit is challenging due to tensions amid the different dimensions (Tura et al., 2018 ). Sustainability needs a balance within the planetary and social limits, whereas development is fueled by growth (ITU, 2017b ). So far, environmental concerns and social divides are poorly covered in the literature. Kreps et al. (2015)  discuss the Ecological World Systems Theory (WST) as a holistic framework to assess the environmental impact of ICT. Indeed, the role of digitalization for sustainability is nascent, based on one-shot philanthropic-based projects or incidental findings from efficiency-focused efforts lacking purpose (Lee, 2014 ; Shin, 2016 ; Watson et al., 2010 ), evidencing a clear research gap. Indeed, research regarding ICTs sustainability is an emerging issue (Yan et al., 2018 ), which was initially focused on narrow quantitative issues, such as energy and carbon footprints, under the term of “Green IT” or “Sustainable ICT”. A strand of literature focuses on ICTs for SD, specifically, on climate change mitigation by reducing carbon emissions and adaptation in urban context (Akande et al., 2019 ). Green IT is supposed to reduce impacts while enhancing sustainable growth contributing towards the SDGs (Imasiku et al., 2019 ). However, technology is not a silver lining and sustainability issues should be pursued from a systemic perspective (Kane et al., 2019 ). Technology and data require governance, security and trust (Letouzé et al., 2019 ). Horner et al. (2016)  review ICT4S taxonomies, concluding that net-impact remains unknown. Thus far, the potential to tackle all sustainability pillars has not yet been fully proved (Bonilla, 2018 ; Madudova et al., 2018 ; Pouri and Hilty, 2018 ). Strong gaps exist regarding value creation, delivery and capture (Parida et al., 2018 ). Nevertheless, social aspects are gaining attention spawning new schools of thought (Fors and Lennefors, 2018 ). A comprehensive analysis should be developed (Sachs et al., 2016 ). Despite the emerging literature, the relationship the topic is not yet fully understood at micro-level. On the other side, sustainability is an imperative for digitalization (Etzion and Aragon-Correa, 2016 ). Without considering sustainability, the digital era would lead to serious threats, increasing vulnerabilities (Helbing, 2012 ). Deprived of a responsible approach, the exponential development of technologies and the singularity expected by 2025, would lead to dire consequences for humankind. Society must accept the responsibility of making technology more sustainable, ethical and human-focused (Leonhard and Graf-von-Kospoth, 2017 ). Therefore, digitalization is central towards sustainability and a sustainable digitalization is possible, it will depend on the way we outline it (Adams et al., 2018 ; Osburg and Lohrmann, 2017 ; WBGU, 2019 ). Digitalization could accelerate sustainable transitions, particularly ecological and social shifts in the industrial domain (Beier et al., 2017 ). However, a number or prerequisites should be ensured to handle potential impacts (Khakurel et al., 2018 ; Pappas et al., 2018 ; Stuermer et al., 2017 ). In addition, dialogue across sectors and actors is needed, since ICTs for sustainability and a sustainable ICT system even when related are different notions (Hofstetter et al., 2020 ). On the whole, the digitalization holds great potential for overcoming research gaps and societal problems, whereas sustainability is also an imperative for a responsible digital transformation. However, current model of digital transformation does not ensure a SD (Chazhaeva et al., 2019 ). A clear gap between technology and sustainability is evidenced (Akande et al., 2019 ). Digitalization brings open sustainability concerns, yet to be disentangled, mainly related to environmental impacts, social divides and ethical issues. A huge space for policy action exist (IEEE, 2018 ), along with a glaring new field of research (Scholz et al., 2018 ), claiming urgent structural change, normative safeguards to shape digitalization towards the public good (Lane et al., 2013 ). Van der Velden (2018b)  suggests reorienting the digital revolution towards sustainability within the planetary boundaries. El Hilali and Manouar (2019)  claim for unleashing sustainability through digitalization. Research and policy should guide a sustainable digital revolution (Pouri and Hilty, 2018 ; Khakurel et al., 2018 ). In conclusion, because of their complex multidimensional nature and nascent state-of-play, the nexus between digitalization and sustainability remains untapped and little is known about how to seize its opportunities while minimizing pitfalls, evidencing manifest research and policy gaps. Consequently, further research about this interplay is needed, as well as new policies and governance approaches.  5.3 The case of smart sustainable cities and communities One of the most promising and widespread applications of ICTs for SD are the smart sustainable cities. Digitalization can be regarded as a tool for sustainable cities (Bifulco et al., 2016 ) following the widely acknowledged framework for smart cities designed by Giffinger and Gudrun (2010)  which comprises 6 dimensions, namely: smart economy, smart mobility, smart environment, smart people, smart living and smart governance. Certainly, as urbanization grows and digitalization permeates the entire society, cities are becoming complex and multidimensional networks, engines of the economic growth which account for the majority of the population and 70% of the greenhouse emissions (Elmqvist et al., 2019 ). Given that city planning becomes critical for the goals (Giles-Corti et al., 2019 ), addressing sustainable and resilient cities by leveraging digital capacities constitutes an imperative for attaining the 2030 Agenda as a whole. An emerging body of literature reveals how urban policy and management could benefit from the digital revolution to foster well-being and eco-innovation, supporting both the “New Urban Agenda” (Valencia et al., 2019 ), the SDG11 (Allam and Dhunny, 2019 ; Amini et al., 2019 ; Balogun et al., 2019 ; Bibri and Krogstieb, 2017 ; Bifulco et al., 2016 ; Croese et al., 2020 ; Kharrazi et al., 2016 ; Vaidya and Chatterji, 2019 ; Valencia et al., 2019 ; Zheng et al., 2020 ), while advancing the rest of SDGs. Ismagilova et al. (2019)  review the alignment of smart cities with the SDGs, underlining limitations and future directions. Amini et al. (2019)  consider smart cities as computationally and socially complex, multi-sectorial, multi-actor and interdependent networks whose objective is promoting SE, quality of life, together with efficient operation and efficient services. Valencia et al. (2019)  underscore the relevance of localisation and transdisciplinary research regarding: boundaries, governance, actors, synergies and trade-offs and indicators. Even when several literature reviews exist in the domain, a lack of systemic quantifiable and multidisciplinary perspective remains (Zheng et al., 2020 ) along with lack of consensus. Indeed, some experts argue that cities cannot be smart and sustainable at the same time (Akande et al., 2019 ) and the potential of digitalization to tackle climate adaptation has not yet been proved (Balogun et al., 2019 ). Moreover, the literature also reveals caveats and complexities. Urban sustainability is still not well understood at policy level (Elmqvist et al., 2019 ), neither is the concept of smart cities, and current indicators for the SDG11 are not consistent, lacking benchmarks (Giles-Corti et al., 2019 ). Implementation issues remain poorly understood, especially concerning resilience (Croese et al., 2020 ). Regarding urban digitalization, multiple open issues persist, such as data privacy and security, misconstructions both on the policy and technology side, along with lack of systemic perspectives able to balance social and environmental issues (Allam and Dhunny, 2019 ; Amini et al., 2019 ; Bibri and Krogstie, 2017 ; Kitchin, 2015 ; Israilidis et al., 2019 ). Kharrazi et al. (2016)  underline the key role of urban Big Data for feeding innovative indicators, thus helping policy-makers to achieve SD. Nevertheless, leveraging the data deluge demands culture and skills advancement, together with investment and dedicated programs. Bibri and Krogstie (2017)  argue that ICT development should be better linked with SD and well-being, while underscoring numerous critical issues which remain overlooked, lacking theory development. Yigitcanlar et al. (2019)  contend that limited evidence exists about the integration of genuine sustainability, since policies are techno-centric and multiple complexities remain, suggesting a knowledge-based development and policy enhancement. Allam and Dhunny (2019)  claim that sustainability, inclusivity and well-being, must not be disregarded. Social and cultural nuances should be integrated, balancing technological, social and environmental factors. Israilidis et al. (2019)  argue that research is mainly focused on technical issues, overlooking social approaches, knowledge-sharing and learning capabilities, conceiving cities as multi-stakeholder and citizen-centered knowledge hub. Michalec et al. (2019)  contend that lack of awareness about how different actors, such as practitioners, understand smart cities, showing that urban climate justice is scarcely embedded in this topic, where policy needs enhancement. Cowley and Caprotti (2019)  regard smart cities as space for experimenting in opposition to critical experts which consider them as top-down imposition for policy-making and planning purposes. Öberg et al. (2007  state that organisational aspects of management are poorly considered, lacking theoretical ground. Despite the limitations and caveats, sustainable, “green” and smart concepts for SD can converge provided that environmental quality and resilience keeps central in policy and planning (Gazzola et al., 2019 ). In conclusion, further research is needed to fully understand the limitations and provide policy and governance recommendations for truly embedded sustainability as a core value and feature of smart cities.  5.4 A first-approach to the role of digital paradigms for pursuing the SDGs, particularly Big Data and Artificial Intelligence Digitalization offers a far-reaching potential on the sustainability arena, for addressing intractable societal, environmental and governance challenges in an unprecedented fashion, towards pursuing the SDGs, as acknowledged by numerous experts (ECOSOC, 2019c ; GeSI, 2019 ; ITU, 2017a ; Sachs et al., 2019b ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ; UN, 2019d ; UNCTAD, 2018 ; UN DESA, 2018 ; UN Global Pulse, 2016 ; World Bank, 2018b ; Walker et al., 2019 ). The nexus between digitalization and the Agenda could be perceived as a “vector of hope” not exempt of threats, demanding transformative shifts (Bissio, 2018 ). Thus, a convergence of both digital and ecological transformation is compelling (Villani, 2018 ), although still untapped (Hampton et al., 2013 ). The digital disruption goes beyond technology, representing an interplay between technology, social change and policy (Hilbert, 2016 ) since socio-economic enablers and understanding the human side are equally important factors (Grech and Camilleri, 2017 ). Relevant synergies among digital paradigms exist, namely Big Data Analytics,2  2 Big Data Analytics refers to the conjunction between huge volumes of data, along with analytics techniques, which make possible to process data into information (CNIL, 2019 ). It could also be interpreted as the conjunction of data crumbs, communities and capacities (Letouzé, 2015 ). Artificial Intelligence refers to the portfolio of technologies, algorithm-based and data-driven computer systems that enable machines and people with digital capabilities such as perception, reasoning, learning and even autonomous decision making (BDVA, 2018 ).   Artificial Intelligence (AI), Internet of Things (IoT), Blockchain or Distributed Ledger Technologies, Quantum Computing, among others. The digitalization era can only be leveraged by articulating all of them together, creating unseen capacities towards accomplishing the SDGs (BDVA, 2017 ; EESC, 2017 ; Kirkpatrick, 2017 ; UN ESCAP, 2017 ). The potential relies on acquiring knowledge from new data sources able to complement and cover limitations of traditional techniques, while enhancing human-capacities, connectivity and collaboration while mitigating subtle risks. Despite its incipient status, a nascent literature revealing the potential of digital paradigms to track progress or advance the SDGs can be found, although unevenly distributed across contexts (UN Women, 2018 ) and lacking a truly comprehensive approach in light on research gaps from both fields. Wu et al. (2018)  review correlations among SDGs and ICTs from practical use cases, identifying research gaps within the three pillars of sustainability. Tjoa and Tjoa (2016)  claim for a holistic approach to include ICTs impacts on the SDGs. ITU (2017b)  outlines risks and opportunities behind ICT for SDGs while the ICT-Benchmark (Huawei, 2017 ) asserts the related correlations. Fernández-Portillo et al. (2019)  underline a positive relationship of ICT and SD in European countries, underscoring the need to enhance connectivity and digital skills. Van der Velden (2018a)  suggests a sustainable digitalization within the Planetary Boundaries and compares two frameworks: the SDGs considering digitalization as a neutral tool and Doughnut Economics, more integrative, able to reveal potential negative impacts. Townsend and Coroama (2018)  plea for a “digital acceleration” of sustainability, underscoring three opportunities: resource efficiency, clean technologies and circularity. The WEF (2020a)  calls for increasing the relevance of digital technologies within the SDGs by aligning the Agenda with the 4th Industrial Digital Revolution through novel platforms. Kostoska and Kocarev (2019)  propose an ICT framework for accelerating the SDGs, considering local and global perspectives, vertical and horizontal relationships amid stakeholders, by integrating data, sustainability and governance. El Massah and Mohieldin (2020)  argue that digital transformation can impact the localization and achievement of the SDGs. Other scholars explore the, yet unknown, relationships and interactions between specific digital paradigms and the SDGs, inter alia: Industry 4.0 (Beier et al., 2017 ; Bonilla, 2018 ; Ghobakhloo, 2020 ; Nhamo et al., 2020 ), IoT (Salam, 2020 ) or Blockchain (Adams et al., 2018 ). Fukuda-Parr and McNeill (2019)  discuss how Big Data heralds disruptive implications for the SDGs governance and indicators. Even when some goals seem more suitable than others for profiting the digital transformation (Letouzé, 2015 ; Pentland, 2015 ; Vinuesa et al., 2019 ), it impacts across all sectors and disciplines (BDVA, 2017 ; ITU, 2017a ; UNCTAD, 2017a ). Indeed, digital paradigms would directly impact targets 9.c, 4.b, 5.b and 17.8, while they could be relevant for realizing all the 17 SDGs (GeSI, 2019 ; UNCTAD, 2017a ). GeSI (2019)  underline that 103 out of 169 SDGs targets are influenced, with potential to benefit by 22%, but also to threat or inhibit some targets. The WEF (2020a)  states that, digital paradigms could impact 10 out of 17 SDGs, enhancing the 70% of their related challenges. However, critical voices denounce a scattered consideration of the digitalization within the Agenda. Novak and Tjoa (2018)  argue that, despite its relevance, none of the SDGs is solely devoted to digitalization and the potential impact of ICTs is poorly considered. Concerning Big Data and AI for the SDGs, their untapped value behind has been addressed by some scholars and practitioners, although scientific literature is still incipient (AI4Good, 2019 ; Alemanno, 2018 ; Chandy et al., 2017 ; Etzion and Aragon-Correa, 2016 ; Guo, 2020 ; Hilbert, 2016 ; ITU, 2019b ; ITU, 2017a ; Lepri et al., 2017 ; Malhotra et al., 2018 ; Seele and Lock, 2017 ; Shiroishi et al., 2019 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ; Song et al., 2017 ; UN Global Pulse, 2016 ; Vinuesa et al., 2019 ; Williams et al., 2019 ). Some initiatives in this realm can be found, namely: the UN Global Pulse (2016) , the UN Open Data Hub (2020) , Data-Pop Alliance (2017) , Datakind (2012) , the UN Global Working Group on Big Data for Official Statistics (2019) , the World Bank Group “Big Data Innovation Challenge” initiative (World Bank, 2016 ), the “ITU4SDG” tools (ITU, 2017c ), the UN World Data Forum from 2017 to 2020 (UNWDF, 2018 ), the World Summit on the Information Society (WSIS, 2019 ), the AI for Good Global Summits from 2017 to 2019 (AI4Good, 2019 ), the “Data for Development” initiative (D4D, 2013 ), the “Data for Good Exchange” from 2014 to 2020 (D4GX, 2014 ) or “Frontier 2030” (WEF, 2020b ). Regardless the relevance of these pioneer studies and initiatives, a profound and holistic analysis of the SDGs gaps, coupled with how ICT paradigms could responsible contribute to bridge them, cannot be found in literature. Indeed, literature reveals that Big Data and AI applied to SD are in its infancy. Little is known yet, about whether it could promote or hinder the realization of the 2030-Agenda. Further multidisciplinary research is needed to fully understand multiple complexities behind this nexus. One of the most salient values of digitalization for the SDGs relies on the data revolution (Jacob, 2017 ; UN-SDSN, 2019 ; ECOSOC, 2019c ). Indeed, relevant challenges remain to grasp timely and high-quality data, along with subsequent analytics (Andries et al., 2019 ; Guo, 2020 ). ICTs are essential to overcome critical data gaps, by complementing traditional data sources and enhancing granularity (Letouze et al., 2019 ), which is particularly problematic in deprived countries (Chandy et al., 2017 ). Novel sources of Big Data such as Earth Observation Data (Andries et al., 2019 ; Guo, 2020 ) or citizens’ mobile data (Malhotra et al., 2018 ) could be useful to feed SDGs indicators and monitoring SD. Certainly, Bullock et al. (2020)  and Oliver et al. (2020)  underline the potential of AI and mobile data for tackling global crisis or pandemic management. People-focused Big Data could enhance SDGs accountability (Williams et al., 2019 ). For instance, digital health technologies enhance the compilation of disaggregated data for the SDG3 (Konduri et al., 2018 ). Given the relevance of interpretation and context-awareness, merging Small and Big Data for feeding better SDGs indicators, creating proxies and complementing official statistics in an affordable way is crucial (UN Women, 2018 ; Van den Homberg and Susha, 2018 ). Data ecosystems could enrich reporting, overcoming fragmentation, divides and data vacuum, enhancing development effectiveness (Letouze et al., 2019 ). Nonetheless, several conditions should be assured for responsibly harnessing the data revolution (UN DESA, 2018 ), namely: ensuring data accessibility and significance; fairness by avoiding bias and capturing diversity; accountability and transparency; cultivating ownership, trust and user-centric data (EU, 2019a ; Fjeld et al., 2020 ; Lepri et al., 2018 ; Letouzé and Pentland, 2018 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ); promoting data stewards (UN Global Pulse-GSMA, 2017 ); securing consensus on data-sharing models (Data-Pop Alliance, 2017 ; ECOSOC, 2018 ); establishing privacy standards while minimizing ethics risks (Campolo et al., 2018 ; Herschel and Miori, 2017 ; EESC, 2017 ; Fjeld et al., 2020 ; Metcalf et al., 2016 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ; Yayboke et al., 2017 ); strengthening statistical systems and building-up digital literacy (Bhargava et al., 2015 ; Letouze et al., 2019 ; Letouzé and Pentland, 2018 ). Moreover, reliable business models, methodologies along with policy for international collaboration and funding are critical factors yet unexploited (UN-SDSN, 2019 ). Notwithstanding, the power of Big Data, AI and synergic ICTs, goes beyond metrics. Indeed, data is unworkable without interpretation (EESC, 2017 ). Digital paradigms provide enhanced capacities for scientific discovering, decision-making, forecasts and governance, becoming the new cornerstone to advance social research (Bright, 2017 ; Fazekas, 2014 ; Paulus et al., 2014 ) and to understand and transform behaviors towards sustainable conducts. The fusion of Big Data Analytics and AI, holds exceptional descriptive, predictive, prescriptive and discursive functions (Letouze, 2015 ), which could contribute holistically across all dimensions of sustainability (Maaroof, 2015 ; Khakurel et al., 2018 ). Thus, digital capacities are essential for addressing complex social problems (Lepri et al., 2017 ; Pappas et al., 2018 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ) so as to enhancing governance towards SD (Athey, 2017 ; Letouze, 2015 ; Lowman, 2017 ). Furthermore, digital paradigms enable novel collaborative digital ecosystems (Jensen and Campbell, 2019 ; Pappas et al., 2018 ; Stuermer et al., 2017 ), expanding global connectivity and facilitating knowledge-sharing (Stafford-Smith et al., 2017 ). However, beyond benefits, digital technologies are also changing the information landscape, raising ethical and social concerns (Fukuda-Parr and McNeill, 2019 ; Metcalf et al., 2016 ; O’Neill, 2016 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ; UN DESA, 2018 ). Big Data disrupts knowledge management and governance, but it could significantly contribute to the SDGs as long as potential threats are carefully managed with proper policy and governance (Fukuda-Parr and McNeill, 2019 ). AI could trigger an ideal world achieving the 2030 Agenda or a dystopian future far for the SDGs (Goralski and Tan, 2020 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ). Regarding ecology issues, they are almost invisible in the related strategies and policies (Villani, 2018 ). Furthermore, sustainable digitalization policies are lacking (Khakurel et al., 2018 ), since only few countries consistently include the SDGs within their AI or digital strategies (Van Roy, 2020 ). Thus their role in pursuing the environmental goals should be enhanced (WEF, 2020a ). Critical voices argue that digitalization challenges outshine opportunities (MacFeely, 2019 ). Hence, since controversies prevail, demonstrating whether and to what extent digital capacities can be used in a responsible, eco-friendly and human-centered fashion towards the SDGs is crucial (Letouze and Pentland, 2018 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ). Nevertheless, so far, a lack of systemic perspectives remains and little is known yet about how such capacities could be achieved without inducing bias or manipulation (Niño et al., 2017 ). Current status is characterized by a fragmented landscape, booming narrowly-focused stand-alone pilots difficult to scale (UN Global Pulse-GSMA, 2017 ) following a reductionist and ambiguous “for good” approach, or even “ethic-washing” tactics (Campalo et al., 2018; Stocker, 2016 ). Multiple barriers persist around policies and regulations, together with lack of tools and insufficient capacities to face the challenges ahead (Lee, 2014 ; Letouzé, 2015 ; Shin, 2016 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ; UN Global Pulse-GSMA, 2017 ; UN Women, 2018 ; Vinuesa et al., 2019 ). A huge gap between potential and performance remains (Chandy et al., 2017 ; Janowski, 2016 ), together with severe limitations to surpassing the philanthropic scope and become mainstream conscientiously. Furthermore, structural challenges remain in terms of, namely: lacking infrastructures, expertise or capacities, quality data, market incentives and regulation, trust and security (WEF, 2020a ). Cybersecurity spillovers could impact SD and persist ignored in research studies (Ahmed, 2020 ). These hurdles cannot be solved by technology alone and are even magnified in deprived contexts (Yayboke et al., 2017 ). Addressing contextual specificities is required (Hesse et al., 2018 ; ITU, 2019b ; Letouzé and Pentland, 2018 ). A massive development of digital technologies without proper governance and means could lead to unsustainable and uneven landscapes. Therefore, digital and sustainability policies should be aligned with the SDGs. Responsible and ethics-driven governance, regulation and means of implementation are urgently needed to ensure a sustainable scale-up (Data-Pop Alliance, 2017 ; EESC, 2017 ; ECOSOC, 2018 ; UN DESA, 2018 ; UNDG, 2017 ; WEF, 2020a ). Building-up digital capacity among governments is crucial for the SDGs implementation (Janowski, 2016 ). Therefore, making the digital revolution works for the SDGs relies on a number of leapfrogs, summarized as follows. Sound enabling frameworks and responsible principle-based regulation are required, together with funding mechanisms, tailored incentives, and sustainable business models allowing collaboration across stakeholders (BDVA, 2017 ; Data4SDGS, 2019 ; Data-Pop Alliance, 2017 ; ITU, 2019a ; ITU, 2017a ; ECOSOC, 2018 ; Letouze, 2015 ; Letouzé and Pentland, 2018 ; Walker et al., 2019 ). Coordinating international efforts among all the ecosystem actors by building-up public-private multi-sectorial partnerships and promoting market demand (UNWDF, 2018 ) is essential (Data-Pop Alliance, 2017 ; ECOSOC, 2018 ; ITU, 2019a ; UN Global Pulse-GSMA, 2017 ; Yayboke et al., 2017 ). Therefore, a profound characterization of the phenomenon is required. Digitalization for the SDGs should be responsibly harnessed for both monitoring and achieving progress, then accelerated and scaled up, unleashing the full potential of ICTs for the Goals. This advancement relies on aligning better ethics-driven policies, sound regulation and enablers, multi-stakeholder alliances and ecosystems, fostering digital literacy, innovation and investments, for driving the Agenda (Sachs et al., 2016 ; WEF, 2020a ).   6 Discussion In light of the literature review and in-depth analysis undertaken, we discuss as follows, the insights gained throughout previous sections 3 to 5 . Firstly, a discussion about the research gaps of the SDGs is provided, mainly in answer to the first research question. Secondly, a preliminary envisage of the contribution of digital paradigms for tackling those gaps and advancing the UN 2030 Agenda is presented, giving response to the second research question. 6.1 Summary of the SDGs research gaps The rationale behind the Sustainable Development Goals has been introduced, uncovering the main research gaps which deserve future attention, summarized along these lines. As analysed in section 3.3 , discussions devoted to the systemic nature of the Agenda and the interlinkages amid goals and targets, have dominated recent research (i.e. Allen et al., 2019b ; Barbier and Burgues, 2017 ; Griggs et al., 2017 ; Liu et al., 2018 ; Liu et al., 2015 ; Nilsson et al., 2018 ; Saito et al., 2017 ; Schmidt et al., 2017 ; Stafford-Smith, 2017 ; Weitz et al., 2018 ). Despite the fast-growing body of literature on this relevant topic, the interlinkages are not fully understood nor soundly integrated within policy, particularly trade-offs (Allen et al., 2019b ; Barbier and Burges, 2019 ; Dolley et al., 2020 ; Kroll et al., 2019 ; Obersteiner et al., 2016 ; Pradhan et al., 2017 ; Scherer et al., 2018 ; Von Stechow et al., 2016 ) and transboundary spill-overs (McCollum et al., 2018 ; Sachs et al., 2019a ; Schmidt-Traub et al., 2019 ).  The SDGs network cannot be considered consistent as argued by Dawes (2019) . Extant studies are fragmented, context-dependent and habitually contradictory. Consensus exists about this flawed understanding, leading to an ill-defined prioritization, which might erode the holistic nature and enactment of the Agenda. Furthermore, multiple complexities (Allen et al., 2018 ; Griggs et al., 2013 ; McCollum et al., 2018 ; Georgeson and Maslin, 2018 ), along with dearth of suitable tools and science-policy interfaces persist (Allen et al., 2018 ; McCollum et al., 2018 ). We evidence the need of further research this major gap, through novel science-based methodologies (Sachs et al., 2019a ; Van Soest et al., 2019 ), able to deal with complexities across contexts and scales by fusing qualitative and quantitative approaches (Karnib, 2017a,b ; Obersteiner et al., 2016 ). In addition, science-policy interfaces (McCollum et al., 2018 ; Obersteiner et al., 2016 ; Zimm et al., 2018 ) and transnational cooperation (Breuer et al., 2019 ; Georgeson and Malin, 2018 ; Nilsson et al., 2016 ; Schneider at al., 2019 ; Weitz et al., 2019 ) would add value as for acknowledging interactions to inform prioritization policies. Combining this analysis with the section 5.4  outcomes, we argue that innovative technologies (Breuer et al., 2019 ; Kroll et al., 2019 ; Schneider et al., 2019 ) and particularly, digital capacities, might be helpful to supercharge scientific discovery and governance towards shedding light on the SDGs interlinkages. Secondly, an emerging strand of literature (see sections 3.4 and 3.5 ) denounce design shortcomings and strong imbalances of the present SDGs scheme, namely: diluted ambition (Fukuda-Parr and McNeill, 2019 ); extreme complexity (Ala-Uddin, 2019 ); inconsistencies (Spaiser et al., 2017 ; Swain, 2018 ) and contradictions (Crespo-Cuaresma et al., 2018 ; Dalby et al., 2019 ; Vasconcellos Oliveira, 2018 ); omitted or underrepresented ecological and human-centric issues (Allen et al., 2016 ; Dalby et al., 2019 ; Gasper et al., 2019 ; Giannetti et al., 2020 ; Gupta and Vegelin, 2016 ; Martinez-Harms et al., 2018 ; Spijkers, 2018 ; Stevens and Kanie, 2016 ; Vasconcellos Oliveira, 2018 ; Weber, 2017 ); unevenness and lack of integration amid dimensions, showing strong financial focus (Ala-Uddin, 2019 ; Lempert, 2017 ; Priyadarshini and Abhilash, 2018 ; Pogge and Sengupta, 2015 ; Scherer et al., 2018 ; Stevens and Kanie, 2016 ; Swain, 2018 ; Weber, 2017 ); absent alignment with widely recognized frames (Lempert, 2017 ; Saner et al., 2019 ); and feeble transformative narrative (Costanza et al., 2016 ; Leal-Filho et al., 2019a ). Prior studies evidence a feeble enactment approach (Diaz-Sarachaga et al., 2018 ; Horton, 2015 ), unsolved tensions across scales and dimensions, along with grand controversies regarding the targets representativeness. These flaws should be tackled since they might erode the credibility and enactment of the Agenda (Georgeson and Maslin, 2018 ; Leal-Filho et al., 2019a ; Saito et al., 2017 ). Thus, an open debate with divergent views about the suitability of the current Agenda scheme is evinced, which might be handled by multidisciplinary science-policy dialogue (Messerli et al., 2019 ) and novel analytical capacities (McCollum et al., 2018 ). Additionally, we observe that emerging sustainability trends, such as conscious capital (Mackey and Sisodia, 2013 ), “net-handprinting” (Kühnen et al., 2019 ), the model of 4 capitals (Ruijs et al., 2018 ) might enrich the debate, although they have been overlooked in the literature. In view of the analysis, it would be highly recommendable to critically revisit the Agenda (Ala-Uddin, 2019 ; Salvia et al., 2019 ; Saner et al., 2019 ; Stevens and Kanie, 2016 ) towards overcoming the unveiled caveats and balancing dimensions (Bergman et al., 2018 ), thus embedding a genuine and holistic SD. Moreover, a relevant body of literature (see section 4.1 ) is devoted to persistent implementation and governance challenges, (i.e. Bartram et al., 2018 ; Caiado et al., 2018 ; Dalby et al., 2019 ; Georgeson and Maslin, 2018 ; Griggs et al., 2017 ; Priyadarshini and Abhilash, 2018 ; Yingchun et al., 2018 ). Most implementation hurdles are closely tied to discerning the localization (Allen et al., 2016 , 2019a bib_Allen_et_al_2019a bib_Allen_et_al_2016 ; Fukuda-Parr and Muchhala, 2020 ; Kabisch et al., 2019 ; Salvia et al., 2019 ; Schmidt-Traub et al., 2019 ; Stafford-Smith et al., 2018 ) and integration matters, together with aforementioned interlinkages. A closer look to recent studies reveals fragmented governance (Monkelbaan, 2019 ), manifold integration issues and lack of comparative studies across contexts (Allen et al., 2016 ; Dickens et al., 2019 ; Elder and Bartalini, 2019 ; Engström et al., 2019 ; Huan et al., 2019 ; Lafortune and Schmidt-Traub, 2019 ; Salvia et al., 2019 ). Such a multidisciplinary Agenda casts, yet unwell understood, complexities (Allen et al., 2018 ; Caiado et al., 2018 ; McCollum et al., 2018 ; Messerli et al., 2019 ) and tensions across scales and dimensions that could lead to unsuitable prioritization (Allen et al., 2018 ; Georgeson and Maslin, 2018 ; Zhou and Moinuddin, 2017 ). Policy caveats persist due to missing whole-of-government approaches and sound integration of contextual issues (Messerli et al., 2019 ). Coordination mechanisms for tackling trans-boundaries issues (Amos and Lydgate, 2019 ; Schmidt et al., 2017 ) merit further development and understanding. Regardless their relevance, only few studies explore the funding gap (Barua, 2019 ; Dalby et al., 2019 ; Lagoarde-Segot, 2020 ). Besides, the roles of capacity-building, awareness-raising (Bowen et al., 2017 ), knowledge management (Mastrángelo et al., 2019 ; Barrantes-Briceño and Almada-Santos, 2019 ), or transformative narratives are poorly developed (Bengtsson et al., 2018 ; Kanie et al., 2019 ; Stevens and Kanie, 2016 ). Hence, these topics deserve further consideration. We observe that multi-level governance (Dalby et al., 2019 ; Martens, 2019 ; Messerli et al., 2019 ; Sachs et al., 2019a ), moving beyond siloes (Allen et al., 2018 ; Dalby et al., 2019 ; Obersteiner et al., 2016 ; Stevens and Kanie, 2016 ), while allowing cross-fertilization (Spijkers, 2016 ) and alignment (Biermann et al., 2017 ; Leal-Filho et al., 2019a ) amongst different levels of governance, disciplines and stakeholders portrays eminent opportunities for bridging hurdles. Nevertheless, little attention has been devoted to these aspects. Therefore, the need of further assessment and novel analytical tools is evidenced (Caiado et al., 2018 ; Sullivan et al., 2018 ), in onder to cope with governance uncertainties and context nuances towards science-based systemic approaches (Bartram et al., 2018 ; Liu et al., 2015 ; Stafford-Smith et al., 2018 ), together with interfaces to inform policies (Georgeson and Maslin, 2018 ; Karlsson-Vinkhuyzen et al., 2018 ; Schmalzbauer and Visbeck, 2016 ). Based on the uncovered gaps, we perceive an opportunity on harnessing digital capacities for addressing a paradigm shift vis-à-vis the aforesaid challenges. Furthermore, policy and financial enabling frameworks are key critical leverage points which would be recommended to further developed, guided by science, innovative and holistic approaches. Prior research (see section 4.2 ) substantiates the importance of measuring progress by monitoring and evaluating the Goals (Dalby et al., 2019 ; Lu et al., 2015 ; Persson et al., 2016 ; Reyers et al., 2017 ; Schmidt-Traub et al., 2017 ; Yonehara et al., 2017 ).Nonetheless, huge research challenges remain regarding accountability (Bebbington and Unerman, 2018 ; Bowen et al., 2017 ; Wackernagel et al., 2017 ; Zimm et al., 2018 ), couple with missing suitable taxonomies (Xu et al., 2020 ), sound methodologies (Crespo-Cuaresma et al., 2018 ) and analytical tools. These limitations are hindering the assessment (Lior et al., 2018 ; Lu et al., 2015 ). Indeed, little attention has been paid to the conflict between accountability and integration (Karlsson-Vinkhuyzen et al., 2018 ), or the dynamics across scales (Xu et al., 2020 ), deserving further exploration to cope with context specificities. Moreover, a context-based assessment going beyond indicators (Barbier and Burgess, 2019 ; Fukuda-Parr and McNeill, 2019 ; Renn et al., 2020 ; Reyers et al., 2017 ; Schönherr et al., 2019 ; Schwandt et al., 2016 ) and capable of reflecting sustainability qualitative and quantitative nuances, while capturing the complexities of the SDGs (Dang and Serajuddin, 2020 ) has yet to be developed. Numerous studies focus on indicators (see section 4.3 ), revealing shortcomings and inconsistencies (Brussel et al., 2019 ), as a result of insufficient scientific foundations and dialogue. Consensus exist on the unsuitability of extant metrics (Annan-Diab and Molinari, 2017 ; Barbier and Burgess, 2019 ; Dlouhá and Pospíšilová, 2018 ; Galli et al., 2018 ; Georgeson and Maslin, 2018 ; Hák et al., 2016 ; Janoušková et al., 2018 ; Lehtonen et al., 2016 ; Ordaz, 2019 ; Salvia et al., 2019 ; Storey et al., 2017 ). Several authors claim for a critical review of present metrics moving beyond GDP-based indicators (Campagnolo et al., 2018 ; Coscieme et al., 2020 ; Costanza et al., 2104 ; Hak et al., 2016; Mair et al., 2018 ; Merry, 2019 ; Rubio-Mozos et al., 2019 ; Saito et al., 2017 ; Schmidt-Traub et al., 2017 ; Suciu and Năsulea, 2019 ). An open debate on alternative indices prevails (Diaz-Sarachaga et al., 2018 ; Fioramonti et al., 2019 ; Lozano et al., 2018 ; Suciu and Năsulea, 2019 ). Moreover, severe limitations persist regarding data availability (Dang and Serajuddin, 2020 ; Jacob, 2017 ; Lozano et al., 2018 ; Martens, 2018 ), interoperability and analytics (Dawes, 2019 ; Hickel, 2019 ) which are hampering progress. As argued by Dang and Serajuddin (2020) , difficulties are not only due to the indicators but come from the goal formulation, which would suggest a refinement to facilitate interpretation. Hence, the analysis illustrates the need of further research efforts on the road to enhanced accountability, assessment approaches and indicators, particularly on Tier II and Tier III indicators (Fukuda-Parr and McNeill, 2019 ) and disregarded transboundary spill-overs (Sachs et al., 2017 ; Schmidt-Traub et al., 2019 ). Given the exceptional prospects that digitalization may bring in this realm (see section 5.4 ), we argue that a full exploration of the role of digital paradigms (e.g. Big Data and AI) to provide novel data sources, proxies and analytics, would add value and help to overcome limitations of Tier II and III indicators. Regarding headway on the SDGs, early-stage literature (see section 4.4 ) unveils truncated adoption, off-target and uneven progress across contexts (Dalby et al., 2019 ; Sachs et al., 2019a ), along with fragmented knowledge. This status might be the result of the confluence of, yet poorly addressed, implementation hurdles (Bryan et al., 2019 ), policy caveats (Moyer and Hedden, 2020 ), deficient capacity and geopolitical megatrends. As argued by Sabau (2020) , scarce progress could also be the consequence of a narrow understanding about sustainability values. Other authors emphasize how overlooking transboundary issues and spill-overs are hindering advancement (Sachs et al., 2019a ; Sachs et al., 2017 ; Schmidt-Traub et al., 2019 ). Present reporting systems have been criticized, since they struggle with data quality, limited consistency (Wackernagel et al., 2017 ) and contradictory outcomes. These grounds suggest the requirement of better aligning the SDGs with existing frames (Biermann et al., 2017 ), developing new benchmarks and ensuring a multidisciplinary perspective (Lozano et al., 2018 ). Accelerating progress is urgently required (Moyer and Hedden, 2020 ; Sachs et al., 2019a ). These arguments suggest the need of further developing data-driven systemic and context-savvy approaches to track the dynamics of progress and nudging adoption. Thus, an opportunity for digital paradigms would emerge in this domain, where digital capacities might be highly supportive. Recent studies emphasize (see section 4.5 ) the relevance of encouraging engagement and ownership among all actors (Galli et al., 2018 ; Lucas et al., 2016 ; Messerli et al., 2019 ; Schönherr et al., 2017 ), along with worldwide collaboration to deliver the Agenda (Dalby et al., 2019 ; Frey and Sabbatino, 2018 ; Kurz, 2020 ). However, research reveals a number of drawbacks in this regard, which might be linked to the unclear roles and responsibilities (Spangenberg, 2017 ) of a non-binding Agenda (Biermann et al., 2017 ), along with persisting silo-based inertias (Bergman et al., 2018 ; Cutter, 2015 ; Dalby et al., 2019 ). Besides, the added-value and means of implementation of the SDGs for every actor (i.e. businesses, academic, civil society, public sector) have not been clearly articulated. Thus, a profound and systematic analysis is required for discerning this relevant topic. A growing body of literature focus on the role of business for pursuing the Agenda, underlining huge potential (Cordova and Celone, 2019 ; Frey and Sabbatino, 2018 ; Martinuzzi et al., 2017 ) but also severe drawbacks in terms of involvement (Vazquez-Brust et al., 2020 ) and yet unknown add-value (Van Zanten and Van Tulder, 2018 ). Some studies have underscored lacking reporting tools and guides (Dahlmann et al., 2019 ; Dalby et al., 2019 ; Lopez, 2020 ; Topple et al., 2017 ), along with uneven alignment between corporate strategies, policy priorities and science perspectives. These shortcomings might lead to cosmetic approaches or counterproductive cherry-picking practices (Pimonenko et al., 2020 ), jeopardizing the purpose-driven holistic vision of the Agenda. Defining a common ground on the sustainability meaning is essential as argued by (Schneider et al., 2019 ). Besides, we consider that exploring novel corporate trends and purpose-driving models would enrich the debate. Regardless its relevance, the role of grassroots and civil society has been assessed only to a very limited extent (Li et al., 2018 ; Martens, 2019 ; Sénit, 2019 ). Little is known about behaviour formation or how to raise public awareness and involvement headed for the Goals (Guan et al., 2019 ; Lucci and Lucci, 2015 ; Rubio-Mozos et al., 2019 ). Further research this topic would be very useful for accelerating progress to the Goals. Furthermore, relevant factors such as cultural influence (Rosati and Faria, 2019 ), barriers to adoption, education (Kopnina, 2020 ) and capacity building remain poorly explored. The literature also underscore the great potential of the collaboration mechanisms and partnerships (Amos and Lydgate, 2019 ; Dalby et al., 2019 ; Florini and Pauli, 2018 ; Horan, 2019 ; Kolk et al., 2017 ; Vazquez-Brust et al., 2020 ) to bring together actors while breaking down siloes. Indeed, the Agenda might be considered a debate platform across stakeholders, as agued by Vildåsen (2018) . Nevertheless, collaboration issues have not been widely explored, showing complexities and governance challenges (Berrone et al., 2019 ; Beisheim and Ellersiek, 2017 ; Melo, 2018 ; Nelson, 2017 ) which merit additional research. Insights drawn from literature evidence that further research is required for a profound understanding of the reported challenges. In view of, the outstanding capacities of digital ecosystems to spur collaboration (Pappas et al., 2018 ), we argue that a novel approach based on digital capacities could enrich this research realm, by reinforcing collaboration, driving narratives for transformation and nudging behaviors towards accelerating progress. Finally, few recent studies (section 4.6 ) cover the essential role of technological innovation diffusion and knowledge transfer for accelerating transformations towards the SDGs. In this respect, current research reveals grand opportunities (Centobelli et al., 2020 ; Colglazier, 2015 ; Cordova and Celone, 2019 ; Walsh et al., 2020 ) but also uncertainties, on top of trade-offs to ecological conservation and social welfare (Aldieri et al., 2019 ). Major knowledge gaps persist in the literature (Barrantes-Briceño and Almada-Santos, 2019 ; Mastrángelo et al., 2019 ). Furthermore, technology and innovation are narrowly considered in the extant Agenda scheme as argued by Walsh et al. (2020) . A number of relevant aspects have been assessed only to a very limited extend, namely: diffusion of spill-overs (Aldieri et al., 2019 ) and knowledge flows; social impacts (Corsi et al., 2020 ); environmental concerns (Capasso et al., 2019 ); responsible research and innovation (Thapa et al., 2019 ; Van der Leeuw, 2018 ; Lehoux et al., 2018 ); complexities and systemic interactions (Labanca et al., 2020 ); inclusive approaches or grassroots innovation. Thus, the aforesaid arguments suggest that these aspects should be further researched and fully embed in policies. Most studies recognize that policy heralds as an essential mediator to overcome potential market or structural failures, impacts and externalities (Aldieri et al., 2020 ; Capasso et al., 2019 ; Sinha et al., 2020 ; Walsh et al., 2020 ) while promoting diffusion of eco-innovation and knowledge-sharing (Berawi, 2017 ). Nonetheless, given the limitations of current policy agendas, several authors claim that they should be reoriented to encourage eco-aware, responsible, inclusive technology-driven transformations towards SD (Centobelli et al., 2020 ; Vatananan-Thesenvitz et al., 2019 ; Walsh et al., 2020 ). Other authors argue that neither technology nor policy alone could facilitate transformations to sustainability (Köhler et al., 2019 ), since additional elements such as strategy, enablers, regulation, competences are required (Rogge and Reichardt, 2016 ; Edmondson et al., 2018 ; Walsh et al., 2020 ). In this realm, digital technologies emerged as essential elements for shaping knowledge (Fukuda-Parr and McNeill, 2019 ) and driving sustainable transformations (Pappas et al., 2018 ). However, literature pays very little attention to this opportunity. In view of the insights reported, we claim that technology and innovation, particularly on the digital realm, are key enablers for catalyzing the SDGs, provided that they are employed in an ethical and eco-friendly fashion. Further research is required to discerning present challenges headed for unlocking technology and innovation for the sake of the Agenda. In conclusion, drawing on the reported results, the authors have clustered seven main research gaps of the SDGs, which deserve further research together with novel approaches. A summary of critical issues and gaps can be found in Table 2   and Fig. 5  . Despite the challenges unveiled, the authors advocate that there exist outstanding opportunities to overcome the aforementioned caveats and accelerate progress towards realizing the Agenda, as illustrated in Fig. 6  . Some of these prospects might rely on harnessing the digital revolution, which is discussed in detail as follows.  6.2 A preliminary envisage on the role of digital paradigms to bridge the SDGs research gaps Drawing on the literature insights (see section 5 ), the second research question is answered as follows. The convergence amid digitalization and sustainability is still a nascent research field, full of opportunities but also controversies. As discussed in section 5.4 , a still incipient body of literature acknowledges the, yet untapped, prospects that digital paradigms might bring to SD (i.e. Chandy et al., 2017 ; Hilbert, 2016 ; Song et al., 2017 ) and towards pursuing the SDGs (i.e. El Massah and Mohieldin, 2020 ; Guo, 2020 ; Kostoska and Kocarev, 2019 ; Letouzé et al., 2019 ; Malhotra et al., 2018 ; Sachs et al., 2019b ; Seele and Lock, 2017 ; Tjoa and Tjoa, 2016 ; Townsend and Coroama, 2018 ; Van der Velden, 2018a ; Vinuesa et al., 2019 ; Walker et al., 2019 ; Williams et al., 2019 ; Wu et al., 2018 ). Indeed, a series of recent studies prove how digital paradigms, predominantly Big Data and Artificial Intelligence, are being incipiently applied for measuring or advancing a relevant number of SDGs (i.e. GeSI, 2019 ; ITU, 2019b ; ITU, 2017a ; Malhotra et al., 2018 ; UN Global Pulse, 2016 ; Vinuesa et al., 2019 ; Walker et al., 2019 ). However, we verify that scientific literature is underdeveloped and fragmented. Most studies come from the grey literature, evidencing that little attention has been paid to the Agenda gaps and whether digital paradigms could contribute to bridge them. In light of the analysis undertaken in section 5.4 , we suggest that the contribution of digital paradigms to bridge the SDGs gaps might be threefold: (1) overcoming extant data vacuum by harnessing non-traditional data sources, while ensuring ground validation, quality and interoperability; (2) providing enhanced data-driven analytical capacities for scientific research, monitoring, assessment, decision-making and governance; (3) expanding collaboration and knowledge-sharing opportunities by building-up highly connected digital ecosystems and data partnerships. The first contribution is widely acknowledged in recent literature. ICTs, and particularly Big Data, seem crucial to compile data and distil it into knowledge (Andries et al., 2019 ; Chandy et al., 2017 ; Fukuda-Parr and McNeill, 2019 ; Guo, 2020 ; Jacob, 2017 ; Konduri et al., 2018 ; Letouze et al., 2019 ; Malhotra et al., 2018 ; Van den Homberg and Susha, 2018 ; Williams et al., 2019 ). Therefore, prior research corroborate that the data deluge brings innovative and affordable paths to overcoming limitations of conventional SDGs metrics, monitoring and assessment process, by merging small traditional data with novel Big Data sources. Nevertheless, the literature also unveils a number of capacity constraints, methodological and market barriers (ITU, 2019b ; ITU, 2017a ; Letouze et al., 2019 ; Walker et al., 2019 ; Yayboke et al., 2017 ), together with social, privacy and ethical concerns (Campolo et al., 2018 ; Herschel and Miori, 2017 ; Lepri et al., 2017 ; Letouzé and Pentland, 2018 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ; Vinuesa et al., 2019 ) which need to be addressed. On these grounds, we claim for ensuring a context-aware, bias-free, trustworthy and ethically-driven approach able to overcome potential risks to sustainability or inclusion. Regarding the second contribution, prior studies illustrate that the genuine added-value of digitalization goes beyond data, which is worthless without interpretation, even more in the multifaceted sustainability realm. The synergic combination of digital paradigms and specifically, the fusion of Big Data Analytics with Artificial Intelligence, yields outstanding computational, analytical and predictive abilities (Letouze, 2015 ; Vinuesa et al., 2019 ) to unprecedentedly supercharge human capacities. Thus, digital capacities push boundaries, having enabled a novel research stream for advancing social and behavioral sciences (Bright, 2017 ; Fazekas, 2014 ; Paulus et al., 2014 ), as well as for enhancing governance of wicked sustainability issues (Lepri et al., 2017 ; Athey, 2017 ; Lowman, 2017 ). Stemming from these insights, we argue that digital capacities would be strongly useful for addressing most of the implementation and governance limitations of the SDGs, based on augmented analytical capacities and data. Nevertheless, prior research seems just a first step and further studies are needed to profoundly understand the extent of their contribution to the Agenda, as well as to tackle potential trade-offs. Concerning the third contribution, despite the relevance of the digital ecosystems for enabling collaboration, trust and knowledge brokering towards SD (Jensen and Campbell, 2019 ; Pappas et al., 2018 ; Stuermer et al., 2017 ), their role towards bridging the SDGs gaps remains poorly explored. Previous studies show that digital paradigms could facilitate connectivity (Fernández-Portillo et al., 2019 ; ITU, 2017a ), knowledge-sharing (Stafford-Smith et al., 2017 ) and collaboration across geographies (ITU, 2017a ; Zheng et al., 2020 ). It would bring together data, capacities and knowledge through data partnerships (Rasche et al., 2019 ; Susha, 2020 ; Yayboke et al., 2017 ). Hence, we substantiate that digital ecosystems might offer remarkable prospects for overcoming limitations of the Agenda related to lacking collaboration and knowledge management (Pappas et al., 2018 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ), while enhancing digital literacy (Bhargava et al., 2015 ; Letouzé and Pentland, 2018 ) and enabling multidisciplinary collaborations (Bibri, 2019 ; ITU, 2017a ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ). Further exploring the contribution of digital ecosystems to the SDGs would enrich the debate. The Fig. 7   as follows summarizes our preliminary approach; outlining the potential contribution of digitalization towards overcoming the previously introduced SDGs research gaps, shedding light to the second research question. Beyond prospects, a closer look to the literature reveals that digital disruption brings also a dark side. Controversies and lack of systemic perspectives remain regarding the sustainability potential of digitalization (Letouze and Pentland, 2018 ; Niño et al., 2017 ). Prior studies corroborate that digital paradigms, specifically Big Data and AI, face unsolved challenges and capacity constraints by themselves, lacking well-developed theoretical and regulatory frameworks (BDVA, 2017 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ). A prominent strand of literature recognizes disruptive governance implications (Fukuda-Parr and McNeill, 2019 ), couple with unintended social, ethical, equity-related and environmental concerns (Wu et al., 2018 ). Thus, a holistic approach for assessing and curbing impacts is required, as argued by Tjoa and Tjoa (2016) . Indeed, intense and inconclusive debates on ethical threats, social divides (Campolo et al., 2018 ; Herschel and Miori, 2017 ; Hilbert, 2016 ; Khakurel et al., 2018 ; Lepri et al., 2018 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ) and dystopias potentially brought by Big Data and AI have dominated recent research (Fukuda-Parr and McNeill, 2019 ; Goralski and Tan, 2020 ; Metcalf et al., 2016 ; O’Neill, 2016 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ). Critical scholars claim that digitalization caveats outstrip opportunities (MacFeely, 2019 ) and actually it conflicts with sustainability (Gebhardt, 2017 ; Unwind, 2017; MacFeely, 2019 ). Moreover, some studies underline a feeble and strewn integration of technology (see section 4.6 ) and digital innovations within the Agenda frame (Novak and Tjoa, 2018 ). This raises the question of whether it would be advisable to explore the convenience of setting clusters for transformation, including one devoted to the digital transformation as suggested by Sachs et al. (2019b) , or even a novel SDG18 devoted to the governance of digitalization and the 4th industrial revolution.
