Thus far, we observe that discussions have been mainly focused on ethical and social concerns, while environmental sustainability has been marginally included (Khakurel et al., 2018 ; Vinuesa et al., 2019 ) or directly ignored in both research and policy agendas (Villani, 2018 ), prevailing a cosmetic approach to SD. We argue that a better integration and fully alignment of digitalization with all dimensions of the SDGs, including ecology issues, might be unavoidable, demanding further research and dialogue. In this regard, it is important to note, that specific complexities emerged when digital paradigms are applied to the sphere of SD. This might be explained, on one hand, because sustainability issues are wicked and featured by tensions amid dimensions. Consequently, achieving net-benefit is challenging by itself (Tura et al., 2018 ). On the other hand, sustainability and digitalization should be pursued from a systemic and ethical perspective, beyond current techno-centric approaches (Hilbert, 2016 ; Kane et al., 2019 ; Leonhard and Graf-von-Kospoth, 2017 ). A closer look to literature evidences a breach between science and practice, along with silo-based disciplines (ITU, 2019b ; ITU, 2017a ), and insufficient diffusion of innovations (see section 4.6 ). These factors might be impeding a mutual understanding and a desirable multidisciplinary approach necessary to merge sustainability and digitalization. This would suggest the need to foster dialogue and collaboration across disciplines (Bibri, 2019 ; Hofstetter et al., 2020 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ; Valencia et al., 2019 ; Zheng et al., 2020 ). Despite the remarkable opportunities ahead, prior and current research reveals that progress in harnessing digital paradigms for pursuing the SDGs is very slow-paced, incremental, and highly context-dependent. Thus, this realm remains at an embryonic and fragmented stage. Furthermore, insights drawn from literature confirm that some SDGs gaps rely on structural, cultural, financial or human factors (ITU, 2017a ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ; Yayboke et al., 2017 Walker et al., 2019 ) which are not susceptible to be solved by technological innovations. ICTs can bring added value, complement and augment human capacity, but another enabling elements are needed, demanding further research. Indeed, previous studies confirm that multiple non-technological and structural barriers persist in terms of policy and regulation (WEF, 2020a ), capacities (Letouzé, 2015 ), infrastructures, market incentives, trust (Letouzé and Pentland, 2018 ; Hesse et al., 2018 ; Shin, 2016 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ) and security (Ahmed, 2020 ; Shin, 2016 ). Hence, a severe gap between potential and realization is evidenced (Chandy et al., 2017 ; Janowski, 2016 ). The predominant school of thought acknowledges the opportunities of digitalization towards pursuing the 2030 Agenda, while claiming for accelerating a responsible, ethics-driven, inclusive and context-aware governance, regulation and enabling frameworks (Kostoska and Kocarev, 2019 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ; Townsend and Coroama, 2018 ; Van der Velden; 2018a ). Particularly relevant are aspects such as fairness, accountability and transparency, as argued by Lepri et al. (2018) . Moreover, collaboration and digital literacy (Bhargava et al., 2015 ; Letouzé and Pentland, 2018 ) across actors, disciplines (Campolo et al., 2018 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ) and geographies would be central to ensure a sustainable scale-up of digital paradigms for the SDGs (Janowski, 2016 ; BDVA, 2017 ; ITU, 2017a ; ITU, 2019a ; Walker et al., 2019 ; WEF, 2020a ). However, we observe a lack of consensus about the notion of ethics, responsibility or “social good” (Berendt, 2019 ; Fjeld et al., 2020 ). Most aforementioned caveats are poorly developed in the scientific literature and insufficiently embedded in policy. We stand at a crossroad for the future of humanity. Devoid of a sustainable approach, the massive development of digital technologies would lead to dire unsustainable landscapes, while without digitalization the SDGs gaps might endure. Digitalization and sustainability are mutually-affective and unavoidable. Incremental improvements of technology are not sufficient to solve sustainability issues, requiring disruptive transformations, as argued by Köhler et al. (2019) . Therefore, identifying the social and environmental value of digitalization for sustainability to scale-up and mainstream this nexus would enrich the debate. Embracing an ethics-driven approach, while mitigating trade-offs and addressing contextual specificities would be highly recommended (Campolo et al., 2018 ; Data-Pop Alliance, 2017 ; EESC, 2017 ; ECOSOC, 2018 ; Fjeld et al., 2020 ; Hesse et al., 2018 ; ITU, 2019b ; Khakurel et al., 2018 ; Letouzé and Pentland, 2018 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ). This approach should cover also ecological concerns, so far overlooked in the research agendas. In light of the insights gained, we might conclude that extant research can only be considered a primary step towards a profound understanding of the underscored challenges and key tenets of this realm. We confirm that digitalization holds outstanding opportunities towards supporting the SDGs, but enhancements in both the Agenda scheme and the current path of digitalization might be advisable towards responsibly scaling-up and harnessing the digital revolution for “social good”. The convergence of digitalization and the SDGs, unfolds a remarkable space for further multidisciplinary research, policy development, dialogue and collaboration amid key actors.  6.3 Preliminary policy implications In light of the discussion presented, the authors envision preliminary policy implications, which should be cautiously interpreted as a first-approach, requiring further exploration. As main implication, the available evidence suggests the need of revisiting and profoundly enhancing the Agenda formulation and related policies to overcome extant shortcomings (Ala-Uddin, 2019 ; Bartram et al., 2018 ; Dang and Serajuddin, 2020 ; Sabau, 2020 ; Saner et al., 2019 ). This seems particularly relevant regarding the novel and cross-cutting goals (i.e. SDG12, SDG17) along with the lagging ones, related to inequality (SDG10) and the environment (SDG13, SDG14, SDG15), since a systemic sustainable approach should keep central (Barbier and Burgess, 2019 ; Spijkers, 2016 ; Stevens and Kanie, 2016 ). The SGDs should serve as narratives for transformation (Horan, 2019 ; Moyer and Hedden, 2020 ; Sachs et al., 2019b ; Stevens and Kanie, 2016 ), encouraging multi-level policy reforms (Dalby et al., 2019 ; Martens, 2019 ; Messerli et al., 2019 ) able to steer a new model of prosperity founded on genuine sustainability (Fukuda-Parr and McNeill, 2019 ). This paradigm shift would attain not only the sustainability sphere, but also all synergic policies (i.e. STI frames), ensuring legitimacy (Donoher, 2017 ; Van der Hel and Biermann, 2017 ), integration and coherence (Collste et al., 2017 ; Dalby et al., 2019 ; Kanie and Biermann, 2017 ; Saito et al., 2017 ; Valencia et al., 2019 ). Thus, a multi-sectoral policy action would be required (Lozano et al., 2018 ) which may be enriched by subsuming novel trends. The breach between science and practitioners, as well as the silos amid disciplines, sectors and countries should be breakdown, by facilitating mutual understanding, cross-fertilization and interdisciplinary collaboration (Allen et al., 2018 ; Bergman et al., 2018 ; Cutter, 2015 ; Dalby et al., 2019 ; Hofstetter et al., 2020 ; Obersteiner et al., 2016 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ; Spijkers, 2016 ; Stevens and Kanie, 2016 ). Therefore, it would be important to encourage a strong integration, alignment, and interdisciplinary coordination amid policies and strategies in the domains of SD (Biermann et al., 2017 ; Leal-Filho et al., 2019a ; Saner et al., 2019 ), digitalization (Pappas et al., 2018 ) and innovation, towards accelerating the SDGs (Van der Velden, 2018a ; El Hilali and Manouar, 2019 ), through suitable mechanisms and incentives (Smith and Neupane, 2018 ). This reinforcement would be undertaken at all scales (Florini and Pauli, 2018 ; Vazquez-Brust et al., 2020 ), across countries (Allen et al., 2016 ; Salvia et al., 2019 ), and throughout the stages, based on comparative benchmarks to context-savvy evaluation (Dickens et al., 2019 ; Giles-Corti et al., 2019 ). Dedicated policy mechanisms for spurring transnational cooperation would be desirable (Breuer et al., 2019 ; Dalby et al., 2019 ; Schneider at al., 2019 ), mainly in order to mediate with tensions and transboundary issues (Amos and Lydgate, 2019 ; Schmidt et al., 2017 ), while overcoming weakened multilateralism and geopolitical risks. An additional significant implication concerns the imperative of acknowledging the SDGs interlinkages and complexities within policy frames (Breuer et al., 2019 ; Obersteiner et al., 2016 ) to avoid unsuitable prioritization and cherry-picking. Having demonstrated that governance should ensure a holistic, context-savvy and whole-of-government approach (Bartram et al., 2018 ; Liu et al., 2015 ; Stafford-Smith et al., 2017 ), this requirement seems to rely on reinforcing the contribution of science technology and innovation (Schneider at al. 2019 ; Van der Hel and Biermann, 2017 ), to inform policy-making, by mutual dialogue, alignment and science-policy interfaces (Collste et al., 2017 ; Liu et al., 2015 ; McCollum et al., 2018 ; Zimm et al., 2018 ). Furthermore, it would be relevant to reinforce knowledge transfer and sharing (Barrantes-Briceño and Almada-Santos, 2019 ; Aldieri et al., 2020 ) and reframe STI (including digital paradigms) by responsible, inclusive, people-centric and environmentally-aware policies, along with suitable diffusion (Centobelli et al., 2020 ; Walsh et al., 2020 ). As argued by Hilbert (2016)  the digital era elicits interplay between technology, social change and policy. Therefore, policy would encourage an ethically-driven digital governance bringing novel methodologies, data sources (Lu et al., 2015 ) and metrics (Vatananan-Thesenvitz et al., 2019 ), in parallel to direct interventions to reinforce infrastructures and capacities (Smith and Neupane, 2018 ). Another relevant aspect to be considered within policy is the combination of top-down and bottom-up approaches, since this diverse approach seems to be beneficial (Karnib, 2017b ; Martens, 2019 ) towards heighten ownership and engagement amid stakeholders, specifically through collaboration (Vazquez-Brust et al., 2020 ) and efficient alliances (Caiado et al., 2018 ; Giannetti et al., 2018 ; Horan, 2019 ). Thus, this would facilitate a whole-of-society approach, unlocking the contribution of civil society and business to the goals. Paving the way towards the goals also relies on social change, raising awareness and action (Bowen et al., 2017 ), building capacity (Horan, 2019 ; Lu et al., 2015 ; Reynolds et al., 2018 ) and reinforcing education and skills (Kopnina, 2020 ), which might be better considered in policy. Particularly, it would be relevant to foster digital literacy (Bhargava et al., 2015 ; Lepri et al., 2018 ; Letouzé and Pentland, 2018 ) among policy-makers and sustainability literacy among digital practitioners in order to facilitate mutual understanding. Moreover, policy would play a crucial role in reinforcing the enabling environment and means of implementation (Bartram et al., 2018 ; Smith and Neupane, 2018 ), along with, levelling the playing field at global and local scale (Bartram et al., 2018 ). This might be materialized by crafting flexible and responsible regulatory and financial frames (Dalby et al., 2019 ; Lagoarde-Segot, 2020 ) able to handle potential threats (Fukuda-Parr and McNeill, 2019 ; Vatananan-Thesenvitz et al., 2019 ; Walsh et al., 2020 ; WEF, 2020a ). Extant enabling grounds should be enhanced, by considering innovative incentives and approaches (Caiado et al., 2018 ; WEF, 2020a ). All in all, we are witnessing a critical momentum for shaping the future of digital and SD strategies. Windows of opportunity are sighted for policy to steer a convergence amid the ecological, social and digital transformation (El Hilali and Manouar, 2019 ; Van der Velden, 2018a ), where digitalization for the SDGs is ethically mainstreamed by mitigating potential risks (Khakurel et al., 2108). The SDGs, and particularly, their ecological and social dimensions, should be strongly embedded in the heart of digital and technological policies and strategies, avoiding cosmetic or “ethic-washing” approaches (Campolo et al., 2018 ; Stocker, 2016 ). Enabling frames and novel incentives would be well received to spur environmental and social accountability of digital paradigms, as climate-friendly and net-sustainable engines.   7 Conclusion In this article we have presented a comprehensive systematic review and profound qualitative analysis of the research field and rationale behind the Sustainable Development Goals within the UN 2030 Agenda, uncovering the main research streams and gaps which deserve further attention (RQ1). Furthermore, this study sheds light about whether, how and to what extent, digital paradigms and ICTs technologies, particularly Big Data Analytics and Artificial Intelligence, could be responsibly harnessed to bridge current SDGs gaps and leapfrog sustainable development (RQ2). Ultimately, the study seeks to provide a general overview and guidance on the SDGs and their nexus with digitalization, unraveling policy implications and recommendations for paving the way towards the Agenda. Thus, our article represents a seminal reference which makes a contribution to further define avenues of research, and facilitate mutual understanding of the convergence of both flourishing domains. Despite the increasing interest and emerging strand of publications on the SDGs, thus far, both the 2030 Agenda and digitalization for SD remain yet untapped domains, revealing numerous open and aspects which have not been fully explored. Furthermore, little attention has been devoted to the role of digital paradigms to deal with the complexities, limitations and research gaps of the 2030 Agenda and consequent policy implications. A holistic categorization of the SDGs challenges and research gaps coupled with the exploration of the contribution of the digital paradigms to leapfrog them has not been produced elsewhere. Hence, the convergence of both realms remains unknown. Our suggested holistic approach is, to our best knowledge, the first of its kind, representing a valuable contribution for determining the current foci and potential directions of further research. Regarding the first research question (RQ1), this article provided a categorization of the SDGS research gaps, by clustering them into 7 main groups. Thereby, the findings evidence that, even when the Agenda embodies an unprecedented opportunity to reconcile progress and sustainability, manifold research and policy gaps persist, which are hindering its universal realization and holistic nature. Severe design shortcomings, along with implementation and governance hurdles have been found and interpreted, concluding that they should be acknowledged and tackled by science, policy and practice. Key findings reveal poor understanding of the complexities across scales and interlinkages among goals and targets, particularly trade-offs and spill-overs. Moreover, results disclose imbalances and tensions amid dimensions, inconsistencies, insufficient attention to contextual nuances and underrepresented elements such as human rights, ecological issues or cultural values. Based on these caveats, a major implication has been revealed: the Agenda should be revisited and profoundly enhance with a genuine holistic ambition. Additionally, strong implementation and governance hurdles persist, which might be explained as a consequence of: lack of integration, unclear responsibilities and reduced ownership amongst actors, limited alignment and localization, poor means of implementation and coordination, along with an absence of suitable enabling frames. Ten years left until the 2030 deadline, progress worldwide is far from ambitions, hindered by huge data vacuum, flawed accountability and assessment methodologies, and unsuitable indicators, silo-based prioritization, along with absence of a genuine narrative of change. Besides, the role of technology, innovation and knowledge management remains underutilized. The findings suggest that these obstacles could be tackled by additional research, direct policy interventions and science-policy dialogue, but most of them demand further innovative approaches yet untapped. Concerning the second research question (RQ2), devoted to the role of digital paradigms towards the Agenda, it is attested that the digital oomph hold both promises and threats for tackling the SDG research gaps. Indeed, digital paradigms and ICTs provide huge amounts of data, unprecedented computational power, novel collaborative ecosystems and enhanced capabilities to cope with wicked societal problems. Particularly, the fusion of Big Data Analytics and Artificial Intelligence bring descriptive, predictive, prescriptive and discursive capacities, which add value to traditional approaches by augmenting human capacity and, overall, enhancing sustainability governance. However, the findings also evidence that digital technologies are not a silver lining and come along with yet unknown caveats, principally ethical issues, social divides and environmental concerns which can jeopardize the SDGs. In addition, the use of digital paradigms for the SDGs is still nascent, showing a fragmented and immature landscape, based on stand-alone pilots, unable to scale and mainstream as a solution to global challenges. Findings evidence that sustainability is neither properly embedded neither within the digital strategies and policies nor in the research agendas, threatening the goals. Furthermore, we found that not all the SDGs challenges can be solved by data-driven solutions or technology-enable capacities. Some hurdles are structural and should be sorted out beyond technology. Additional direct interventions enhancing the playing field are required, and should be guided by ethical and inclusive policy, providing regulatory and financial enablers. Our study reveals implications for theory, practice and policy. In light of the findings, we claim that, achieving the SDGs will require profound science-guided and technology-enabled policy reforms and strong alignment of the realms of SD, technological innovation and digitalization. This entails adopting a holistic and responsible approach, huge concerted efforts across contexts, actors and disciplines, together with technology, policy and social enablers. Appealing narratives for transformation are also needed, able to jointly engage policy makers, business, academics and the whole civil society on the way to this blueprint for humanity and the planet. In terms of limitations, firstly we underline that both sustainability and digitalization are multidimensional, highly complex, and ever-evolving realms, featured by lacking sound theory development, consensus definitions and unclear delineation of research boundaries. Both the SDGs, as well as the convergence of digitalization and sustainability, embody nascent and multidisciplinary areas of research, where the scientific literature has not being developed at the same pace as the phenomena in study. Thus, the literature presents an immature, fragmented landscape, with manifold controversies and unexplored issues. This circumstance is exacerbated in the digitalization domain, evidencing glaring gaps in the scientific literature, which have been covered by grey literature from renowned practitioners and multilateral organisations. Such a multidisciplinary domain demands to integrate all relevant perspectives to portray the phenomenon in its whole magnitude. Given the aforesaid limitations, this research should be cautiously interpreted as first-approach exploration. Besides, due to the emerging nature of the topics and the methodological approach based on manual screening and qualitative analysis, we acknowledge that some relevant studies could have been unintendedly omitted. Concerning future avenues of research, this study can be regarded as a seminal reference which illustrates the SDGs features and their nexus with digitalization. Regardless the relevance of the findings generated, this article only scratches the surface of a huge and untapped space for research. We corroborate that further exploration coupled with policy and practice development is required in every research gap and implication uncovered in this review. We confirm that this multifaceted field offers promising avenues of further multidisciplinary research in order to fully understand and leverage the convergence amid sustainability and digitalization towards tackling the SDGs challenges. We advocate that the humanity has an outstanding opportunity to ensure a responsible and inclusive digital transformation which contributes to the fulfilment of the 2030 Agenda.
